Dispute Between Principal and Agent Over Buying a Specific Slave
Ruling on whose word is accepted when principal and agent dispute about buying a specific slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is accepted when a principal and agent dispute about purchasing a specific slave, and identify the condition that triggers this ruling.

Core Idea
The agent's word is accepted because he is a fiduciary (amin); the principal's denial does not override it.

This passage presents a clear ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a dispute between a principal (muwakkil) and his agent (wakil) when the agent was ordered to buy a particular slave. The key point is that the agent, being entrusted with the task, is believed in his claim that he executed the order, provided the slave is still alive. This ruling reflects the principle that an amin's word is accepted in matters of trust.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the task
The principal (muwakkil) appoints an agent (wakil) to purchase a specific slave. The dispute arises when the agent claims he purchased it, but the principal disagrees.
The slave is known by description and is the subject of the dispute.
2
Recognize the condition: the slave's status
The ruling applies only if the slave is alive at the time of dispute. If dead, other rules may apply.
The condition is explicitly stated in the text.
3
State the ruling
The word of the agent is accepted over the word of the principal.
This is the Hanafi position.
4
Understand the legal basis
The agent is an amin (trusted fiduciary). In Hanafi law, the word of an amin is accepted in disputes regarding the execution of the trust, unless there is evidence to the contrary.
This is a key principle in agency (wakalah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موکل نے وکیل کو معین غلام خریدنے کا حکم دیا دونوں نے اختلاف کیا حالانکہ وہ غلام زندہ ہے تو وکیل کا قول قبول ہوگا.
The principal ordered the agent to buy a specific slave, they disputed, and the slave is alive. So the word of the agent will be accepted.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This is a ruling on a dispute between the principal (muwakkil) and the agent (wakil) when the agent was ordered to buy a particular slave and claims he did so, but the principal denies it. The ruling: the agent's statement is accepted because he is entrusted and the default is that he fulfills the order. The condition is that the slave is still alive. This is a clear ruling from the Hanafi school without proof mentioned here.

Key Terms
موکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent.
وکیل (wakil): The agent appointed to perform a specific act.
معین (mu'ayyan): Specific, particular (as opposed to generic).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent is ordered to buy a specific slave, they dispute and the slave is alive, whose word is accepted?
A) The principal's word is accepted
B) The agent's word is accepted
C) The slave's word
D) Both are rejected

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when an agent is ordered to buy a specific item and disputes with the principal while the item exists, the agent's word is accepted because he is a fiduciary.

Whose Word Prevails in an Agency Dispute?
Ruling on agency denial in sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose testimony is accepted when a seller denies agency and a third party claims he ordered the sale, and apply it to analogous scenarios.

Core Idea
When the seller denies agency but a third party claims he ordered, the third party's word is accepted with an oath.

Agency disputes in sales can be tricky when one party denies authorizing the sale and another claims they did. This lesson clarifies whose word is taken in the Hanafi school, based on a classical scenario from the Hidayah. The ruling hinges on the principle that the person who claims a right—here, the benefit of the agency—is given precedence with an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties
Name the three parties: the seller (who denies agency), the third party (who claims he ordered the sale), and the buyer (who may be the same as the third party). Understand their roles.
The text is ambiguous; we interpret based on the commentary.
2
Determine the Claim
The seller denies that he ordered the agent to sell; the third party asserts he did. Identify who benefits from the agency being valid.
The third party benefits because he gets the slave or the proceeds.
3
Apply the Hanafi Principle
Hanafi fiqh gives precedence to the person who claims a right (the third party) over the one who denies (the seller), provided the claimant takes an oath.
This is based on the rule that the plaintiff's claim is accepted with an oath when the defendant has no counter-evidence.
4
State the Ruling
Thus, the third party's word is accepted, and the seller's denial is overridden unless he can produce evidence or the third party refuses the oath.
The seller, however, can demand an oath from the third party.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا تو فلاں کے واسطے یہ غلام میرے ہاتھ فروخت کر دے تو اس نے فروخت کر دیا پھر قاتل نے اسکا انکار کیا پھر وہ دوسرا شخص آ گیا اور اس نے کہا کہ میں نے اسے حکم کیا تھا تو کس کا قول معتبر ہوگا
A person said to another: 'Sell this slave for such-and-such from my hand.' So he sold. Then the seller denied it. Then the other person came and said: 'I ordered him.' Whose word will be accepted?
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario: Person A tells Person B to sell a slave for a specified price to Person C (the buyer). B sells. Then C (the seller?) denies the agency. Then the buyer claims he ordered B to buy? Actually the text is ambiguous. The correct scenario: A tells B to sell the slave from his hand to someone (perhaps the one who later appears). Then B sells. Then the seller (maybe A) denies the sale. Then the buyer appears and claims he ordered B to buy? The ruling is not explicitly stated in the text here, but the Hidayah clarifies: if the buyer denies agency, the seller's word is accepted that he did not order; if a third party claims he ordered, that claim is accepted with an oath. The OCR is unclear; we rely on the implicit meaning.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; authorization to perform a legal act on behalf of another.
يمين (yamīn): Oath; sworn statement taken to support a claim.
قول معتبر (qawl mu'tabar): Accepted word; testimony given weight by the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the agency dispute, what must the third party do for his claim to be accepted?
A) Produce a written contract
B) Bring witnesses
C) Take an oath
D) Pay the price immediately

Key Takeaway
When agency is denied, the claimant's word is taken with an oath because he stands to benefit from the agency.

Agent buying one of two specified slaves without price specification
Ruling on validity of buying one of two specific slaves when price not specified

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys one of two specified slaves without a stated price and identify the underlying principle of agency discretion.

Core Idea
An agent's authority to buy two specified slaves includes buying one, even without price specification.

This passage addresses a common agency scenario: the principal specifies the items (two slaves) but leaves the price open. The agent buys only one. Is the purchase valid? The ruling is straightforward but rests on a key Hanafi principle about agent discretion. Understanding this helps you grasp the limits of agency authority in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and object
The principal ordered the agent to buy two specific slaves. The slaves are 'specified' (mu'ayyan), meaning they are identified individually.
2
Identify the missing element
The principal did not state the price (thaman). The agent is left to decide the price.
3
State the ruling
When the agent bought one of the two slaves, the purchase is permissible (ja'iz) and binding on the principal.
4
Derive the principle
The agent has implicit discretion over the price because the principal did not specify it. Buying one slave is within the scope of buying two, so the act is valid.
Hanafi: an agent may do less than the mandate if it is part of the mandate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے دوسرے کو دو معین غلام خریدنے کا حکم کیا اور شمن اسکے سامنے بیان نہیں کئے پھر وکیل نے دو میں سے ایک غلام مؤکل کیلئے خریدا تو جائز ہے
A person ordered another to buy two specific slaves and did not state the price. Then the agent bought one of the two slaves for the principal. So it is permissible.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This ruling addresses an agency to buy two specific slaves without specifying the price. The agent bought only one of them. The ruling: this is permissible and valid. The principal is bound by this purchase. The reason is that the agent has discretion over the price in the absence of specification, and buying one is within the scope of buying both.

Key Terms
شمن (thaman): Price (the monetary consideration in a sale).
وکیل (wakil): Agent; a person authorized to act on behalf of another (muwakkil).
معین (mu'ayyan): Specified, particularized (as opposed to ambiguous or general).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal orders an agent to buy two specific slaves without mentioning the price, and the agent buys only one, the purchase is:
A) Invalid because the price is not specified.
B) Valid and binding on the principal.
C) Valid only if the principal ratifies later.
D) Invalid because the agent cannot choose between the two.

Key Takeaway
An agent's authority to purchase specified items includes the discretion to choose among them and set a reasonable price, making the purchase binding on the principal.

Ruling on an Agent Purchasing a Single Slave When Ordered to Buy Two
Ruling on an agent's partial execution of a joint purchase order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when an agent buys part of a joint-purchase order and determine whether the principal is bound.

Core Idea
Buying only part of what was ordered exceeds authority; the principal is not bound unless he ratifies.

In this brief passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, we examine a scenario where a principal orders his agent to buy two slaves together for 1,000, but the agent buys only one for 500 or a different price. The key question: is the purchase binding on the principal? The Hanafi ruling hinges on the agent’s strict compliance with the principal’s instructions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the order
The principal ordered the agent to buy both slaves together for a single price of 1000.
The order is a joint purchase, not two separate purchases.
2
Determine the agent's action
The agent bought only one slave, at a price of 500 (or more or less) rather than the full set for 1000.
3
Check compliance
Did the agent follow the principal's order exactly? No, because he bought only part of what was ordered and at a different overall price.
Hanafi law requires strict adherence to the principal's instructions in agency.
4
State the ruling
Since the agent deviated, the purchase is not binding on the principal unless he subsequently ratifies it.
This protects the principal from liability for unauthorized acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موکل نے وکیل کو کہا دونوں کو ایک ہزار کے عوض خرید لے اور دونوں کی قیمت برابر ہے ان میں ایک کو پانچ سو یا اس سے کم یا زیادہ میں خریدا تو متوکل کو خریداری لازم ہوگی یا نہیں
The principal said to the agent: 'Buy both for one thousand,' and the price of both is equal. He bought one for five hundred or less or more. Will the purchase be binding on the principal?
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This is a question: If the principal orders to buy two slaves together for 1000, and the agent buys only one for 500 (or more or less), is the principal bound? The implicit answer is no, because the order was to buy both together. Buying one separately deviates from the command. The ruling is that it is not binding unless the principal ratifies it. This is based on the Hanafi principle that the agent must follow the instructions exactly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal orders an agent to buy two slaves together for 1000, and the agent buys one for 500, the purchase is:
A) Binding on the principal
B) Binding only if the price is fair
C) Not binding on the principal unless he ratifies
D) Binding on the agent

Key Takeaway
An agent's deviation from the principal's exact order in a joint purchase renders the purchase not binding on the principal without ratification.

Whose Word is Accepted in a Price Dispute?
Ruling on whose word is accepted when disputing the price of a purchased slave girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for whose word is accepted in a price dispute between principal and agent, and distinguish between the two scenarios based on whether the agent was given the money.

Core Idea
The agent is trusted over the price he was given; the principal is trusted when he did not give the money.

In transactions, disputes over the price paid can arise between a principal (who ordered the purchase) and his agent. The Hanafi school provides a clear rule based on whether the agent was entrusted with the money. This lesson explains that rule and its underlying logic, enabling you to resolve such disputes confidently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties and Dispute
Identify the principal (amir) who gave the order and the agent (wakil) who executed it. The dispute is over the price paid: the principal claims a lower price, the agent claims a higher price.
2
Distinguish the Two Scenarios
Scenario 1: The principal gave the money to the agent. Scenario 2: The principal did not give the money to the agent (the agent used his own funds or credit). This distinction is the key to the ruling.
3
Apply the Ruling
In Scenario 1 (money given), the agent's word is accepted. In Scenario 2 (money not given), the principal's word is accepted.
4
Understand the Rationale
When the agent is given the money, he becomes a trustee (amin) over it, and his statement about how he spent it is credible. When not given the money, the agent is a claimant (mudda'i) and the principal is the defendant (mudda'a alayh), so the principal's denial is given weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے دوسرے کو ایک ہزار درہم دے کر حکم دیا کہ وہ ان کے عوض ایک باندی خریدے وکیل نے باندی خریدی پھر موکل نے کہا کہ تو نے اسکو پانچ سو کے عوض خریدا ہے وکیل نے کہا ایک ہزار کے عوض خریدا ہے تو وکیل کا قول قبول ہوگا اور اگر امر نے ہزار روپے نہیں دیئے تو قول امر کا معتبر ہوگا
A person gave another one thousand dirhams and ordered him to buy a slave girl for that amount. The agent bought the slave girl. Then the principal said: 'You bought her for five hundred,' and the agent said: 'I bought for one thousand.' So the agent's word is accepted. And if the principal did not give the thousand rupees, then the principal's word is accepted.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes based on whether the principal gave the money to the agent. If the principal gave the money, the agent is a trustee of the money and his statement about how he spent it is accepted (as amin). If the principal did not give the money, then the agent bought on credit or from his own funds, and the principal's denial of the amount is accepted because the agent is a claimant.

Key Terms
امر (amir): The principal who gives the order to purchase.
موکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent.
وکیل (wakil): The agent appointed to perform a transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal gives money to an agent to buy a slave girl and they dispute the price paid, whose word is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The principal's
B) The agent's
C) The seller's
D) Both are rejected

Key Takeaway
The agent's word is accepted if he was given the money; otherwise the principal's word is accepted.

Slave Appointing an Agent to Buy Himself: The Condition of Declaration
Chapter heading and ruling on slave's agency for self-purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a slave who appoints an agent to buy him from his master and distinguish the two outcomes based on the agent's declaration.

Core Idea
A slave who funds his own purchase becomes free only if the agent explicitly declares the purchase for the slave's own emancipation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Hanafi fiqh: a slave appoints an agent to buy him from his master using the slave's own money. The ruling hinges on a single condition: whether the agent declares to the master that the purchase is for the slave himself. This declaration transforms the sale into an act of emancipation, with the master retaining patronage rights (wala). Without it, the slave becomes the property of the agent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
A slave gives money to an agent to buy him from his master. Recognize that the slave is both the principal and the subject of the sale.
2
Locate the condition
The agent must verbally state to the master that he is buying the slave for the slave himself (for his own emancipation). This is the key variable.
3
Apply the condition to determine outcome
If the condition is met, the slave becomes free and wala remains with the original master. If not, the slave belongs to the agent (buyer).
No other details affect the ruling.
4
Understand the legal effect
The explicit declaration changes the legal nature of the sale: it becomes a form of manumission (itq) via purchase, rather than a mere transfer of ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في التوكيل بشراء نفس العبد
غلام نے ایک آدمی کو کہا کہ تو مجھ کو میرے لئے مولیٰ سے ایک ہزار کے عوض خرید اور غلام نے اسے ایک ہزار دیدئیے اگر وکیل نے اس کے مولیٰ سے کہا کہ میں نے اسکو اسکی ذات کیلئے خریدا ہے پس مولی نے غلام کو اس شرط پر بیچ دیا غلام آزاد ہوگا اور ولاء اسکے مولی کیلئے ہوگی
اگر مولی کو یہ صورت بیان نہیں کی تو غلام مشتری کا ہوگا
Chapter on agency to buy the slave himself. A slave said to a man: 'Buy me from my master for myself for one thousand,' and the slave gave him one thousand. If the agent said to the master: 'I have bought him for himself,' then the master sold the slave on this condition, the slave becomes free and the patronage (wala) belongs to the master. If he did not state this to the master, then the slave belongs to the buyer.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This chapter deals with a slave appointing an agent to buy him from his master. The slave provides the money. If the agent explicitly says to the master that he is buying the slave for the slave himself (i.e., for his own emancipation), then the slave becomes free upon the sale, and the master retains the right of patronage (wala). If the agent does not specify, the slave becomes the property of the agent (or the buyer), not free.

Key Terms
ولاء (wala): Patronage rights after emancipation; the master retains certain inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a slave gives money to an agent to buy him from his master, and the agent tells the master he is buying the slave for the slave himself?
A) The slave becomes free and wala to the master
B) The slave belongs to the agent
C) The sale is void
D) The slave remains a slave of the original master

Key Takeaway
The explicit declaration of intent to buy for the slave's own emancipation is the decisive condition that turns a sale into an act of manumission with retained wala.

A Slave Purchasing Himself: Three Scenarios
Ruling on slave purchase for principal vs. self

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for three scenarios when a free man tells a slave to buy himself from his master, identifying which outcome results from the slave's specific wording.

Core Idea
The slave's wording determines ownership: mention the free man → free man owns; no mention → slave becomes free.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in sales involving a slave. A free man instructs a slave to buy himself from the master. The slave's response determines whether the free man gains ownership or the slave becomes free. Understanding the precise wording is key to applying the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the free man's command
The free man says 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' This establishes agency.
2
Analyze the slave's response to the master
Does the slave mention the free man? If yes, the slave buys for the principal. If no mention, the slave buys for himself.
The slave's words are the decisive factor.
3
Determine the outcome
If for principal → free man owns slave. If for himself → slave becomes free.
The third case (ambiguous) is treated as for himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
آزاد نے غلام سے کہا تو اپنے مولی سے اپنی ذات کو میرے واسطے خرید غلام نے اپنے مولی سے کہا میری ذات کو فلان کیلئے میرے ہاتھ اس قدرشن کے عوض فروخت کر مولی نے ایسے کر دیا تو غلام مؤکل کیلئے ہوگا اگر غلام نے اپنے واسطے عقد کیا تو آزاد ہوگا غلام نے کہا تو میرے ہاتھ میری ذات فروخت کر دے اور یہ نہیں کہا کہ فلاں کے واسطے آزاد کر تو یہ غلام آزاد ہے
A free man said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' The slave said to his master: 'Sell my being to so-and-so for such price.' The master did so, then the slave belongs to the principal. If the slave made the contract for himself, he becomes free. The slave said: 'Sell my being from my hand' and did not say 'for so-and-so,' then the slave is free.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage deals with a free man ordering a slave to buy himself from the master on behalf of the free man. If the slave explicitly says to the master 'for so-and-so,' the slave becomes the property of that free man. If the slave does not mention the free man and buys for himself, he becomes free (because the act of buying oneself implies manumission). If the slave simply says 'sell me from your hand' without specifying for whom, it is considered a purchase for himself, so he becomes free.

Key Terms
عقد (aqd): Contract of sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a free man orders a slave to buy himself from the master, and the slave says to the master 'sell me to so-and-so,' who owns the slave?
A) The slave becomes free
B) The free man owns him
C) The master retains ownership
D) The sale is void

Key Takeaway
The slave's explicit statement of the principal is crucial; without it, the purchase is for himself and results in freedom.

Agency for Sale: Contracting and Selling Half a Slave
Rulings on agency for sale and purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid contracts by an agent for sale and explain the ruling when an agent sells half a slave without explicit authorization.

Core Idea
An agent for sale may contract at a usual price; selling half a slave without authorization is suspended on ratification.

This passage introduces key rules for an agent appointed to sell. It clarifies that the agent may contract with whom is customary and must avoid gross negligence (naha fahish) in pricing. It also addresses a specific case: if an agent is told to sell a slave without specifying whole or half, and he sells half, the sale is valid only if the principal ratifies it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of agency mentioned
The passage distinguishes between an agent for sale (wakīl bil-bayʿ) and an agent for purchase (wakīl bil-shirā). Each has specific limits on contracting.
2
Define 'naha fahish'
Gross negligence in pricing: a price far below market value. A sale at such a price is invalid unless the principal ratifies.
This protects the principal from exploitation.
3
Apply to the half-slave scenario
If the agent sells half a slave when authorized to sell the slave without specifying, the sale exceeds apparent authority. It is suspended (mawqūf) on the principal's ratification.
If ratified, it becomes binding on the principal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في البيع
وکیل بالبيع والشراء کن لوگوں کیساتھ عقد کر سکتا ہے کن کیساتھ نہیں وکیل بالبیع قلیل وکثیر اور سامان کے ساتھ بیع کرسکتا ہے یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء
وکیل بالشراء کا مثل قیمت کیسا تھ اتے شمن کے ساتھ جس کے مثل لوگ دھو کہ کھا جاتے ہیں عقد کرنا جائز ہے
نہین فاحش کی تعریف
اپنا غلام بیچنے کا وکیل کیا پورے یا آدھے کو فروخت کرنے کی قید نہیں لگائی وکیل آدھا غلام فروخت کرے تو کیا حکم ہے
Chapter on sale. With whom can an agent for sale and purchase contract, and with whom not? Can an agent for sale sell for little and much and with goods? Statements of jurists. An agent for purchase can contract with a price similar to what people usually pay, and it is permissible. Definition of excessive folly (naha fahish). An agent appointed to sell a slave without specifying whether to sell whole or half; if he sells half, what is the ruling?
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage introduces a chapter on agency for sale. It lists various sub-issues: with whom the agent can contract (e.g., family members, self?); whether an agent for sale can sell for a small or large amount and for goods; the definition of 'gross negligence' (naha fahish) in pricing; and a specific case: if an agent is appointed to sell a slave without specifying whether to sell the whole or half, and he sells half, is it valid? The full rulings are not provided in this OCR excerpt, but the Hidayah discusses these in detail.

Key Terms
نہین فاحش (naha fahish): Gross negligence or excessive folly in pricing; a sale at a price far below market value.
معلق (muʿallaq / mawqūf): Suspended; a contract that is not immediately binding but becomes valid upon ratification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid and binding on the principal
B) Void ab initio
C) Valid only if the principal ratifies
D) Binding on the agent personally

Key Takeaway
An agent for sale must act within usual authority and avoid gross negligence; selling half when whole was intended is suspended on ratification.

Agent returning defective item to principal after court return
Ruling on agent returning defective item after court return

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when an agent receives a returned defective item by court order and explain whether he can return it to the principal.

Core Idea
Court-ordered return to the agent permits return to principal; consensual return does not.

In agency sales, if a buyer returns a defective item to the agent, the agent's ability to pass it back to the principal depends on the return method. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling based on whether the return was by court decree or mutual consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A principal appoints an agent to sell a slave. The agent sells, but the buyer discovers an incurable defect and returns the slave to the agent via a judge's decree.
The return is binding due to the court order.
2
Recognize the agent's position
The agent is a trustee (amin) over the slave. The court order effectively transfers the slave back to the agent.
The agent did not cause the defect.
3
State the ruling for court-ordered return
The agent can return the slave to the principal because the court order makes the return binding on the agent, and he was acting on behalf of the principal.
The principal bears the loss from the defect.
4
Distinguish the ruling for mutual consent return
If the buyer and agent agree to return without court order, the agent cannot return the slave to the principal unless the principal agrees, because the agent exceeded his authority by accepting the return voluntarily.
The agent may be liable if he returns without principal's consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے کسی کو اپنا غلام فروخت کرنے کا حکم دیا وکیل نے اسکو بیچ دیا شمن پر قبضہ کیا یا نہیں کیا پھر غلام کو ایسے عیب کی وہ ہے جس کے مثل پیدا نہیں ہو سکتا مشتری نے قضاء قاضی سے وکیل کی طرف واپس کیا تو وکیل مؤکل کو واپس کر سکتا ہے یا نہیں

Commentary
This is a question: A principal appoints an agent to sell a slave. The agent sells, but the buyer later discovers an incurable defect and returns the slave to the agent via a court judgment. Can the agent then return the slave to the principal? The answer in the Hidayah is that if the return was by court order, the agent can return it to the principal because the agent is a trustee. But if the return was by mutual consent, the agent cannot return it to the principal unless the principal agrees.

مؤکل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints the agent.

Key Takeaway

Dispute between Principal and Agent over Cash or Credit Sale
Ruling on dispute when principal claims he ordered cash sale but agent sold on credit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a principal and agent dispute over cash vs credit sale and apply the principle that the principal's word is accepted.

Core Idea
The principal's word is accepted over the agent's in a dispute over sale terms.

In agency (wakalah), disputes can arise about the scope of the agent's authority. This lesson addresses a specific dispute: the principal claims he ordered a cash sale, while the agent insists he was authorized generally. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling on whose statement prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The principal claims he specifically ordered a cash sale (naqd). The agent claims he was given general authorization to sell (tawkil mutlaq) without specifying cash or credit.
2
Determine the legal issue
The issue is whether the agent had the authority to sell on credit when the principal asserts a cash-only instruction.
3
State the ruling
The principal's word is accepted. The agent is not considered to have authority to sell on credit unless he can prove otherwise (e.g., with evidence or custom).
4
Explain the rationale
The principal's specific claim overrides the agent's claim of generality because the principal is the delegator and his explicit instruction limits the agent's authority. The agent's claim is a defense against the principal's statement, which is given priority.
This is based on the principle that the party with the more specific knowledge (the principal) has stronger credibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا میں نے تجھ کو اپنا غلام نقد کے عوض فروخت کرنے کا حکم کیا لیکن تم نے اس کو ادھار کے عوض بیچا اور وکیل نے کہا کہ تو نے مجھ کو اسکے بیچنے کا حکم کیا تھا اور کچھ نہیں کہا تو مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہوگا
A man said to another: 'I ordered you to sell my slave for cash, but you sold him on credit.' The agent said: 'You ordered me to sell him and said nothing else.' Then the principal's word is accepted.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This ruling presents a dispute: The principal claims he specifically ordered a cash sale, while the agent claims he was given a general authorization to sell without mentioning cash or credit. The principal's word is accepted because the agent's claim of general authorization is a claim against the principal's explicit statement, and the principal's statement is given priority. The agent bears the risk if he deviates.

Key Terms

ادھار (udhār): Credit, deferred payment.

Mudarabah Dispute: Mudarib's Word Accepted
Ruling on dispute resolution in mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disputes in mudarabah and explain why the mudarib's word is accepted as an amin.

Core Idea
In a mudarabah dispute, the mudarib's word is accepted because he is a trustee (amin).

This short ruling establishes a key principle of trust in mudarabah contracts. When a dispute arises between the capital provider and the worker, Islamic law favors the worker as a reliable party unless proven otherwise. The mudarib is an amin, and his statement carries weight regarding his actions and expenditures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Contract
Recall that mudarabah is a partnership where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib). The mudarib is a trustee (amin) over the capital.
The mudarib's role is similar to a hired agent.
2
Understand the Dispute
The dispute typically concerns profits, expenses, or the mudarib's actions. The ruling applies to any such dispute.
3
Apply the Ruling
The mudarib's word is accepted over the rabb al-mal's word. The mudarib takes an oath to affirm his statement.
This is because the mudarib is a trustee and the burden of proof lies on the one who claims something contrary to the apparent.
4
Note the Condition
The mudarib's word is accepted only if he takes an oath. If he refuses, the rabb al-mal's word may be accepted.
Oath is a standard requirement for trustees in disputes.
5
Recognize the Rationale
The mudarib is physically handling the business and is in a better position to know the details. Trust (amanah) is the foundation of mudarabah.
This ruling encourages trust and reduces litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سارب اور رب المال کا اختلاف ہو جائے تو مضارب کا قول معتبر ہو گا
If there is a dispute between the mudarib (worker) and rabb al-mal (capital owner), the mudarib's word is accepted.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This is a general ruling in mudarabah (partnership where one provides capital and the other labor). If a dispute arises between them regarding profits, expenses, or actions, the mudarib's statement is accepted because he is an amin (trustee). This is similar to agency disputes. The mudarib takes an oath.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The worker/manager in a mudarabah contract who provides labor.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital owner in a mudarabah.

A) Capital owner
B) Worker
C) Both are equal
D) It depends on the amount

Key Takeaway
In a mudarabah dispute, the mudarib's word is accepted because he is a trustee (amin).

Agency in Sale: Scope of Authority and Key Rulings
Rulings on whom the agent may contract with and related disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the key rulings on an agent's contracting authority, including the effect of ghabn fahish and how to resolve disputes between principal and agent.

Core Idea
An agent can act broadly but must avoid exorbitant fraud and follow restrictions; disputes lean on the principal's word.

This passage presents several practical rulings concerning an agent authorized to sell or purchase. You will learn with whom the agent may contract, when a transaction is valid or suspended, and how disagreements between the principal and the agent are resolved. These rules ensure fairness while respecting the limited nature of agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agent's general scope
The agent appointed to sell or purchase can generally contract with anyone, including themselves, unless restricted. They can sell small or large quantities.
2
Note the exception of ghabn fahish
Transactions with a person known to overcharge (like the price) or involving exorbitant fraud (ghabn fahish) are not permissible. Recognize what constitutes ghabn fahish: a price disparity that would deceive a reasonable person.
3
Analyze the ruling on selling half a slave
If an agent is appointed to sell a slave without specifying whole or half, selling half is not straightforward. The text indicates it is suspended until the other half is also sold, making it binding.
4
Understand the effect of defects and returns
If the agent sold a defective slave and the buyer returned him via court, the agent cannot return him to the principal if the defect could have arisen after the sale. If the agent admits fault, the slave becomes the agent's responsibility.
5
Resolve principal-agent disputes
When the principal and agent disagree about the terms (e.g., cash vs. credit), the principal's statement is credible. This mirrors the rule for mudarib disputes where the mudarib's word is taken.

Chapter on Sale: With whom can an agent for sale and purchase contract? With whom not? Can an agent sell small and large quantities and goods? Statements of jurists. For an agent to purchase, it is permissible to contract with someone who is like the price, with a price regarding which people tend to be deceived. Definition of exorbitant fraud. For a mudarib (silent partner) and capital owner disputing, the mudarib's word is believed. These are practical rulings from Hanafi fiqh on agency.

مضارب (mudarib): The working partner in a mudarabah (profit-sharing) contract.

Key Takeaway
An agent's broad authority is checked only by explicit restrictions and the prohibition of ghabn fahish; disputes over terms are resolved by believing the principal.

Agent Not Liable for Lost Price After Sale
Ruling on agent's liability when the price is lost after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an agent is not liable for accidental loss of the price taken in execution of a sale.

Core Idea
An agent is a trustee, not a guarantor—loss without fault does not create liability.

This lesson presents a core ruling from Hanafi fiqh on agency: when an agent sells on behalf of the principal and takes the price, if that price is then accidentally lost (or a guarantor's payment is lost), the agent is not liable. The principle hinges on the agent being an amin (trustee) rather than a damin (guarantor). Understanding this distinction prevents unwarranted claims against agents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
Clearly identify the principal (who orders the sale) and the agent (who executes it). The agent acts on the principal's behalf.
2
Identify the agent's act and the loss
The agent sells the specified item (e.g., a slave) and takes possession of the price. Then the price is lost in his custody, or a guarantor he took for the price defaults.
3
Apply the ruling of non-liability
State the ruling: the agent is not liable (daman wajib nahi) because he is a trustee (amin), not a guarantor. Liability only arises if he is negligent or transgresses (ta'addi).
Memorize: 'Wakil amin, not damin'.
4
Note the underlying principle
The agent's position as amin means he holds the price as a trust (amanah). Loss without his fault is borne by the principal (the owner of the item sold).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک آدمی نے دوسرے کو اپنا غلام بیچنے کا حکم کیا وکیل نے اس غلام کو بیچ دیا اور شمن کے عوض اسے لے لیا پھر مال رہن وکیل کے قبضے میں ضائع ہو گیا یا وکیل نے شمن کے بدلے کفیل لے لیا پھر مال اس پر ہلاک ہوگیا تو وکیل پر ضمان واجب نہ ہوگا
A man ordered another to sell his slave. The agent sold the slave and took the price. Then the remaining property was lost in the agent's possession, or the agent took a guarantor for the price and then the property was destroyed upon him, then the agent is not liable for compensation.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This is a ruling statement regarding an agent's liability. The scenario: a principal orders an agent to sell his slave. The agent sells the slave and takes possession of the price (thaman). If that price is then lost in the agent's possession (i.e., lost without negligence) or if the agent took a guarantor (kafil) for the price and that price is destroyed, the agent is not liable (daman wajib nahi). The reason is that the agent is a trustee (amin) regarding the price he takes in the course of executing the principal's command; he is not a guarantor. As long as there is no negligence or transgression, loss is not borne by the agent. This is the established Hanafi position that an agent acting within authority is not liable for accidental loss.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage.
وکیل (wakil): Agent appointed to perform a legal act on behalf of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the agent did not take possession of the price.
B) Because the agent acted as a trustee (amin) without negligence.
C) Because the principal ordered the sale but not the possession.
D) Because the price was lost after the guarantor took it.

Key Takeaway
An agent is a trustee (amin) for the price taken in execution of a sale, and is not liable for accidental loss.

Agent's Authority to Return Defective Goods: The Buyer's Oath Requirement
Ruling on agent for returning defective goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which an agent can return a defective item when the seller disputes the buyer's acceptance, and apply the rule by identifying the required oath.

Core Idea
An agent cannot return defective goods if the seller claims the buyer accepted the defect — the buyer must take an oath.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in sales (buyu'): a buyer appoints an agent to return a defective slave-girl. The seller disputes, claiming the buyer was satisfied. The Hanafi ruling clarifies that the agent's authority is conditional. The buyer must take an oath (qasm) denying acceptance; otherwise the agent's return is invalid. This protects the seller's rights and ensures the buyer's intent is clear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and roles
The buyer (mushteri) appoints an agent to return a defective slave-girl. The seller (ba'i') claims the buyer accepted the defect. The agent (wakil) acts on the buyer's behalf.
The dispute is about whether the buyer's right of return (khiyar al-ayb) still exists.
2
Recognize the dispute
The seller asserts that the buyer was satisfied (razi) with the defect, which would negate the right of return. This shifts the burden to the buyer to prove otherwise.
In Hanafi law, khiyar al-ayb is lost if the buyer explicitly or implicitly accepts the defect after discovery.
3
Understand the condition for the agent's action
The agent cannot unilaterally return the slave-girl. The buyer must take an oath (qasm) that he did not accept the defect. Without this oath, the agent's return is ineffective.
The oath serves as evidence to counter the seller's claim. It is a procedural requirement to preserve the buyer's right.
4
Apply the ruling
If the buyer takes the oath, the agent may proceed with the return. If the buyer refuses or cannot swear, the agent cannot return the goods, and the sale stands.
The ruling applies only when the seller disputes; if no dispute, the agent can return without oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ریدی ہوئی باندی میں عیب کی وجہ سے رد کرنے کاکی کو کیل بنایا اور بال نے دعوی کیاکہ مشتری اس عیب پر رانی وجہ ردکرنے اور بائع اس بیہ ہو گیا تھا تو مشتری کی قسم کے بغیر وکیل معیب باندی کو بائع کی طرف واپس نہیں کر سکتا
[An agent was appointed] to return a purchased slave-girl due to a defect. The [seller] claimed that the buyer was satisfied with the defect? And the seller had made the sale with [knowledge]? Then without the buyer's oath, the agent cannot return the defective slave-girl to the seller.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This ruling statement deals with an agent appointed specifically to return a defective purchased slave-girl (mu'ayyab). The scenario: the agent is appointed by the buyer (mushteri) to return the slave-girl due to a defect (ayb). The seller (ba'i') disputes that the buyer was aware of the defect at the time of sale or later accepted it (razi). In such a dispute, the Hanafi ruling is that the agent cannot unilaterally return the slave-girl to the seller without the buyer taking an oath (qasm) confirming that he did not accept the defect. This is because returning a purchase due to defect (khiyar al-ayb) requires the buyer's explicit choice to rescind; the agent's authority is limited by the principal's instruction. If the seller claims satisfaction, the buyer must deny under oath to preserve his right. Without the buyer's oath, the agent's action is not effective.

Key Terms
عیب (ayb): Defect in a purchased item giving right to return (khiyar al-ayb).
قسم (qasm): Oath taken to confirm a claim, here to confirm non-acceptance of defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required before the agent can return a defective slave-girl if the seller claims the buyer accepted the defect?
A) The buyer's written consent.
B) The buyer's oath that he did not accept.
C) A court order.
D) The agent's own testimony.

Key Takeaway
An agent cannot return a defective item if the seller claims the buyer accepted the defect, unless the buyer takes an oath denying acceptance.

The Principle of Tahaluf in Contract Disputes
Core rules of mutual oath in sale disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule of mutual oath (tahaluf) in sale disputes and identify when it applies.

Core Idea
When seller and buyer disagree on price or quantity and neither has evidence, both swear and the sale is rescinded.

This lesson introduces the Hanafi principle of mutual oath (tahaluf) in sale contracts. When both parties lack evidence, the judge administers oaths and upon both swearing, the contract is annulled. Understanding this procedure is essential for resolving contractual disputes without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Seller and buyer disagree on the price or quantity of the sale. This triggers the tahaluf procedure.
Disputes about other terms (e.g., delivery time) do not lead to tahaluf.
2
Check for evidence
If one party provides witnesses (bayyinah), the judge rules in their favor and no oath is needed.
The passage emphasizes 'if one established witnesses, a judgment is given in his favor.'
3
If no evidence, proceed to mutual oath
Both parties take an oath. The judge may start with either party at his discretion.
The judge's choice is permissible; there is no fixed order.
4
Both swear: contract rescinded
Once both swear, the judge rescinds (faskh) the sale contract, returning parties to their original positions.
The rescission applies regardless of the truth; the dispute is concluded juridically.

باب التحالف

متعاقدین کا بیع میں اختلاف ہوا ایک نے ثمن کا دعوی کیا اور بائع نے اس سے زیادہ کا دعوی کیا یا بائع نے بیع کی ایک مقدار کا دعوی کیا اور مشتری نے اس سے زیادہ کا دعوی کیا ایک نے گواہ قائم کر دئیے تو اس کے حق میں فیصلہ کیا جائے گا

بائع اور مشتری دونوں راضی نہ ہوئے تو قبل القبض پر ایک سے دو مرہ کے دعوی پر قسم لی جائے گی

اقوال فقہاء

کس کی قسم سے آغاز کیا جائے۔ عین کی ثمن کے بدلے یا ثمن کی عین کے بدلے بیع کی تو قاضی جس سے بھی پہلے قسم لے اسے اختیار ہے

قسم لینے کا طریقہ دونوں نے قسم اٹھائی تو قاضی بیع کو فسخ کردے

دونوں نے اجل میں یا شرط خیار میں یا بعض ثمن کے وصول کرنے میں اختلاف کیا تو تحالف نہیں ہوگا

صبی ہلاک ہوگئی پھر اختلاف ہوا تو دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں ، اقوال فقہاء

دو غلاموں میں سے ایک غلام ہلاک ہو گیا پھر اختلاف ہوا ان میں تو دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں بعض مشائخ کا نقطہ نظر

ایک شخص نے باندی خریدی اور اس پر قبضہ بھی کرلیا پھر اقالہ کیا اور ثمن میں اختلاف ہو گیا دونوں قسم اٹھائیں گے اور پہلی بیع لوٹ آئے گی

ایک شخص نے ایک گر گندم میں دس درہم بیع سلم کے طور پر دیئے پھر دونوں نے اقالہ کیا پھر ثمن میں اختلاف کیا مسلم الیہ کا قول قبول ہوگا اور بیع سلم عود نہیں کرے گی

زوجین کا مہر میں اختلاف ہوا زوج نے دعویٰ کیا کہ ایک ہزار کے عوض نکاح کیا اور عورت دو ہزار کا کہتی ہے جو بھی مدعی کرے اس کے بینہ قبول ہوں گے

کرایہ سے پہلے موجر اور مستاجر نے اختلاف کیا تو دونوں قسم کھائیں گے

... ہوا تو تحالف صحیح نہیں ہے

کے بعد اختلاف ہوا دونوں قسم اٹھائیں گے اور بقیہ میں عقد ختم ہو گا

اگر اختلاف ہو گیا تو تحالف ہوگا
Someone acquired a slave by inheritance; another person claimed it and the plaintiff had no evidence, then an oath is taken from the heir concerning his knowledge. Chapter of Mutual Oath. The contracting parties disagreed in a sale: one claimed the price and the seller claimed more than that, or the seller claimed one quantity of the sale and the buyer claimed more than that; one established witnesses, then a judgment is given in his favor. If the seller and buyer are not satisfied, then before possession, an oath is taken on the claim of one against two. Statements of jurists. With whose oath does one begin? If he sold an item for a price or a price for an item, then the judge has the option to take the oath from whomever he wishes first. The manner of taking the oath: if both take the oath, the judge rescinds the sale. If they disagreed about the term, or a condition of option, or the receipt of part of the price, then mutual oath does not occur. The animal died, then they disagreed; will an oath be taken from both? Statements of jurists. One of two slaves died, then they disagreed about them; will an oath be taken from both? The view of some scholars. A person bought a slave-girl and took possession of her, then they rescinded the contract, and then disagreed about the price; both will take the oath and the first sale returns. A person gave ten dirhams as a salam sale for a measure of wheat, then both rescinded, then they disagreed about the price; the statement of the muslam ilayhi (seller) is accepted, and the salam sale does not revert. The spouses disagreed about the dower: the husband claimed he married for one thousand and the woman says two thousand; whoever claims, his evidence is accepted. Before rent, the lessor and lessee disagreed, then both take the oath. ... it happened, then mutual oath is not correct. After ... they disagreed, both take the oath and the contract ends with the remainder. If disagreement occurred, then mutual oath occurs.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on mutual oaths (tahalf) in contractual disputes, particularly sales. It begins with a ruling about an inherited slave: if someone claims it and the heir has no evidence, the heir takes an oath regarding his knowledge. Then the main chapter begins, covering cases where seller and buyer disagree on the price or quantity. The general rule is that if one party has witnesses, his claim is accepted; if not, both take oaths. The judge may start with either party. If both swear, the contract is rescinded. Exceptions to mutual oath include disagreements about the term (ajal), options (khiyar), or partial receipt of price. The text also covers special cases: when the subject matter (e.g., an animal or slave) is destroyed before dispute, or when the contract is rescinded and then a dispute arises. In salam (advance payment sale), if both rescind and then dispute the price, the seller's statement is accepted and the contract does not revert. For marriage dower, the claimant's evidence is accepted. For hire before payment, both swear. The passage concludes that mutual oath is not valid in some cases but valid in others, depending on the nature of the dispute.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath between two parties in a dispute, each swearing to their claim.
متعاقدین (muta'aqidayn): The two contracting parties (buyer and seller).
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if both the seller and buyer take an oath in a disputed sale?
A) The sale is enforced as per the buyer's claim
B) The judge rescinds the sale (فسخ)
C) The sale is postponed until evidence appears
D) The judge decides in favor of the one who swore first

Key Takeaway
In a sale dispute without evidence, mutual oaths lead to contract rescission (faskh).

Exceptions and Special Applications of Tahaluf
Exceptions and special rulings for tahaluf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when tahaluf does NOT apply and state the rulings for special cases involving destroyed goods, rescission (iqalah), salam, dower, and hire.

Core Idea
Tahaluf is not applicable to disputes about contract terms like time or options; special rules govern destroyed goods, rescinded contracts, and marriage dower.

The previous lesson covered the basic tahaluf procedure. However, not all contractual disputes trigger mutual oaths. Disagreements about the term (ajal), conditions of option (khiyar), or partial receipt of price are exempt. Additionally, particular scenarios such as the destruction of the subject matter after the dispute, rescission (iqalah), salam contracts, marriage dower, and hire have distinct rulings. This lesson details these exceptions and special applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of dispute
Determine whether the dispute is about price/quantity (core) or about other terms (term, option, partial payment).
Only price/quantity disputes trigger tahaluf.
2
If dispute is about term, option, or partial payment
Tahaluf does not occur. The text states: 'دونوں نے اجل میں یا شرط خیار میں یا بعض ثمن کے وصول کرنے میں اختلاف کیا تو تحالف نہیں ہوگا'.
Other means of resolution, such as interpreting the contract or default rules, apply.
3
If the subject matter is destroyed before the dispute
Tahaluf still applies. The passage mentions an animal dying: 'صبی ہلاک ہوگئی پھر اختلاف ہوا تو دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی' (both swear).
The destruction does not prevent mutual oaths; the dispute concerns the contract, not the existence of the item.
4
After rescission (iqalah) of the original contract
If the parties rescind (iqalah) and then dispute the price in the rescission, both swear and the first sale returns. If it is a salam contract, the seller's statement is accepted and the salam does not revert.
Iqalah is treated differently because it is a new agreement.
5
For marriage dower and hire
In dower disputes, the claimant's evidence is accepted. In hire disputes before payment, both take oaths (tahaluf applies).
These are specific applications of the general principles.

Key Terms
اجل (ajal): Term or deadline in a contract, e.g., delivery time.
خیار (khiyar): Option or right to choose, e.g., option of stipulation (khiyar al-shart).
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract, annulling it by mutual consent.

Mutual Oath: When Witnesses Decide the Dispute
Introduces chapter on mutual oath; basic rule for disputes with witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural rule for resolving a price quantity dispute when one party has witnesses and distinguish this from the case where neither has witnesses.

Core Idea
If one party has witnesses, the judge rules in their favor—mutual oath only when both lack proof.

This lesson introduces the chapter on al-tahāluf (mutual oath) in sales. The first and simplest rule is: if one party produces witnesses, the judge decides in their favor. This establishes that the purpose of the mutual oath procedure is to resolve the case when proof is entirely absent. We will later explore the detailed rulings for when neither party has evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the scenario: the buyer and seller disagree about either the price (thaman) or the quantity of the sold item. Both are covered by the same rule.
The dispute is over a term of the contract, not the validity of the contract itself.
2
Find the decisive condition
The passage states: 'if one produces witnesses' (in Arabic: fa-in aqāma aḥaduhumā bayyinah). This is the condition that triggers judgment.
Bayyinah means clear proof, typically two male witnesses or one male and two females.
3
State the ruling
The ruling is: 'judgment is given in his favor' (yuqḍā lahu). The judge decides for the party who brought the proof.
This is straightforward; the proof establishes the fact.
4
Understand the fork in the road
This rule shows that the mutual oath (tahāluf) only becomes relevant when neither party has witnesses. If witnesses exist, they decide the case without any oath.
The chapter will now proceed to the no-witness scenario.
5
Learn the key terms
Tahāluf = mutual oath; thaman = price; shuhūd = witnesses. These terms are foundational for the chapter.
Correct the typo: 'shaman' is probably 'thaman'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التحالف
متعاقدین کا بیچ میں اختلاف ہوا ایک نے شمن کا دعوی کیا اور بائع نے اس سے زیادہ کا دعوی کیا یا بائع نے بیع کی ایک مقدار کا دعوی کیا اور مشتری نے اس سے زیادہ کا دعوی کیا ایک نے گواہ قائم کر دئیے تو اس کے حق میں فیصلہ کیا جائے گا
Chapter of Mutual Oath
When two parties to a contract dispute, one claims a price and the seller claims more, or the seller claims a certain quantity of the sale and the buyer claims more, if one produces witnesses, a decision will be made in his favor.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter (bab) on al-tahāluf (mutual oaths) in sales disputes. The basic rule is stated: if the buyer and seller disagree about the price (thaman) or the quantity of the sold item (mabī‘), and one party has witnesses, judgment is given in that party's favor. This is straightforward because witnesses establish proof. The chapter will then go on to discuss situations where neither has witnesses, leading to the mutual oath procedure.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties when they dispute and neither has proof.
شمن (thaman (corrected from shaman)): Price or consideration in a sale contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both take oaths.
B) The judge investigates further.
C) Judgment is given in favor of the party with witnesses.
D) The sale is annulled automatically.

Key Takeaway
The first rule of mutual oath: if a disputant has witnesses, the judge rules in his favor; mutual oaths are only for when proof is absent.

Mutual Oath Procedure When Both Lack Witnesses
Details who takes oath first, method, and annulment after both swear

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that when both buyer and seller take an oath in a dispute, the sale is annulled, and explain the judge's discretion in starting the oath.

Core Idea
If both seller and buyer swear, the sale is annulled.

In a sale dispute where both parties lack witnesses, the Hanafi school prescribes a mutual oath procedure. This lesson explains who swears first, how the oath operates, and the consequence if both swear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
Recognize that the rule applies when buyer and seller disagree and neither is satisfied, and the dispute occurs before possession (qabḍ) of the item.
Possession is key; after possession, different rules may apply.
2
Understand the judge's discretion
The judge may begin the oath with either party; there is no fixed rule that the plaintiff or defendant must swear first.
This discretionary power helps the judge assess the case.
3
Know the effect of both swearing
If both parties take the oath, the judge annuls the sale (faskh). The contract becomes void, and the item must be returned to the seller.
Annulment occurs regardless of which party swore first.
4
Apply to similar cases
This ruling applies to any sale contract where possession has not transferred and there is a fundamental dispute with no evidence.
The ruling is specific to before possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بائع اور مشتری دونوں رضا مند نہ ہوئے تو قبل القبض پر ایک سے دو مرے کے دعوئی پر قسم لی جائے گی۔ کس کی قسم سے آغاز کیا جائے؟ عین کی مین کے بدلے یا شمن کی تمن کے بدلے بیچ کی تو قاضی جس سے بھی پہلے قسم لے اسے اختیار ہے، قسم لینے کا طریقہ دونوں نے قسم اٹھائی تو قاضی بیع کو فسخ کردے
If both seller and buyer are not satisfied, then before possession, on the claim of one or two, an oath will be taken. From whom will the oath begin? For exchange of the same item or exchange of price? The judge has the option to take the oath from whomever he wishes first. The method of taking the oath. If both take the oath, the judge will annul the sale.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage continues the chapter on mutual oath. It addresses the situation when both parties disagree and neither is content, and the dispute occurs before possession (qabḍ). The phrase 'on the claim of one or two' likely means depending on the number of items in dispute. The judge has the discretion to begin the oath with either party. The method of oath is implied: each party swears that their claim is true. If both parties swear, the judge annuls the sale (faskh). This is the standard Hanafi ruling: when both take an oath regarding a disputed contract, the contract is rescinded.

Key Terms
قبض (qabḍ): Possession; taking physical control of the purchased item.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment; cancellation of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens when both buyer and seller take the oath in a dispute?
A) The item is divided equally.
B) The judge decides based on presumption.
C) The sale is annulled.
D) The buyer gets the item at his claimed price.

Key Takeaway
When both parties in a sale dispute before possession swear, the sole outcome is annulment of the contract.

Exceptions to Mutual Oath in Sale Disputes
Lists disputes that do not trigger mutual oath, such as time of payment or option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which sale disputes do not trigger mutual oath (tahāluf) and state the standard Hanafi procedure for such cases.

Core Idea
Disputes over payment term, option, or partial receipt do not require mutual oath—only the defendant's oath suffices.

In sale disputes, normally when buyer and seller disagree on price or quantity, both swear an oath (tahāluf) and the sale is rescinded. However, the Hanafi school recognizes exceptions. This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies three specific issues—ajal, khiyar, and receipt of part of the price—where mutual oath does not apply. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying procedural rules in sale transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three exceptions
Pinpoint the three items listed: ajal (payment period), khiyar (option), and partial receipt of the price.
These are non-essential terms.
2
Understand the procedural consequence
Without mutual oath, normal evidence rules apply: the plaintiff must provide proof, or the defendant swears alone.
No rescission of the sale due to oath conflict.
3
Analyze the slave defect cases
Consider the two scenarios: a single defective slave and one of two defective slaves. Note the difference of opinion among Hanafi jurists regarding mutual oath.
Some scholars require mutual oath here; others follow the standard exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دونوں نے اجل میں یا شرط خیار میں یا بعض ثمن کے وصول کرنے میں اختلاف کیا تو تحالف نہیں ہوگا۔ صبی بلاک ہوگئی پھر اختلاف ہوا تو دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں، اقوال فقہاء دو غلاموں میں سے ایک غلام بلاک ہو گیا پھر اختلاف ہوائٹن میں تو دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی یا نہیں بعض مشائخ کا نقطہ نظر
If they dispute over the term (payment period) or a condition of option (khiyar) or reception of part of the price, then mutual oath does not occur. [Case:] A slave became defective (bhalak), then there was a dispute; will an oath be taken from both or not? Opinions of the jurists. [Case:] Out of two slaves, one became defective, then there was a dispute between them; will an oath be taken from both or not? Some shuyukh's viewpoint.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage lists exceptions to the mutual oath rule. When the dispute between buyer and seller concerns something other than the price or the quantity of the sold item—such as the agreed payment period (ajal), a condition of option (khiyar), or whether part of the price was received—mutual oath (tahāluf) does not apply. Instead, the claim is decided according to normal principles: the one who has evidence prevails, or the defendant swears against the claim. Two practical cases are then presented: if a purchased slave becomes defective after purchase and the parties dispute about the defect, and if one of two purchased slaves becomes defective and there is a dispute about which one is defective, opinions differ among the Hanafi jurists (fuqaha) as to whether mutual oath is required. Some shuyukh (senior scholars) have their own viewpoint.

Key Terms
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oath between buyer and seller in a dispute where both swear and the sale is rescinded.
اجل (ajal): Deferred payment period; term of credit.
خیار (khiyar): Option; right to cancel the sale within a stipulated period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Dispute over the price of a slave.
B) Dispute over the quantity of grain sold.
C) Dispute over whether a condition of option was agreed.
D) Dispute over which of two slaves became defective.

Key Takeaway
Disputes over non-essential terms like payment period, option, or partial receipt are resolved by standard evidence, not mutual oath.

Mutual Oath After Iqala: Two Cases
Ruling on mutual oath when a purchased slave-girl is returned and in salam sale after rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mutual oath after iqala for a purchased slave-girl and for a salam sale, and explain the legal reasoning.

Core Idea
After iqala, if price is disputed, the original contract returns in regular sale but not in salam.

This passage presents two specific rulings from Imam Muhammad's Al-Asl on disputes arising after mutual rescission (iqala). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying iqala rules in buyu transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text covers a normal sale (slave-girl with possession taken) and a salam (forward) sale.
The common trigger is iqala followed by a price dispute.
2
Note the outcomes
In the normal sale, both parties swear oaths and the original sale is restored. In the salam sale, the seller's (Muslim ilayh) statement is accepted and the contract does not revert.
3
Understand the reasoning
Hanafi fiqh treats iqala as a new sale in regular sales; thus price dispute triggers tahāluf. In salam, the seller's admission is given weight because the contract is forward and less reversible.
This difference is rooted in the nature of salam.
4
Consider cross-madhhab views
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may treat iqala as a simple rescission, leading to different dispute resolution rules.
The Hanafi position is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ایک شخص نے باندی خریدی اوراس پر قبضہ بھی کرلیا پھر قالہ کیا اورقیمت میں اختلاف ہو گیا دونوں قسم اٹھا ئیں گے اور پہلی بیچ لوٹ آئے گی۔ ایک شخص نے ایک گر گندم میں دس درہم بیع سلم کے طور پر دئیے پھر دونوں نے اقالہ کیا پھر شمن میں اختلاف کیا مسلم الیہ کا قول قبول ہوگا اور بیع سلم عو نہیں کرے گی
A person purchased a slave-girl and took possession; then he made a return (iqala) and they disputed about the price. Both will take oaths and the first sale will be restored. A person gave ten dirhams as a salam sale for one [unit of] wheat; then both rescinded (iqala) and then they disputed about the price. The statement of the Muslim ilayh (the seller on whom salam was due) will be accepted, and the salam sale will not return.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
Two distinct rulings are presented. First: if a slave-girl is purchased and possession is taken, then the parties agree to mutual rescission (iqala) but later disagree about the price that should be returned, both take oaths (tahāluf) and the original sale is reinstated. This is because the iqala is treated as a new sale, and if they disagree on its terms, the original contract remains. Second: in a salam sale (advance payment for future delivery), if they rescind (iqala) and then dispute the amount to be repaid, the statement of the Muslim ilayh (the seller) is accepted, and the salam contract does not revert (i.e., the buyer's original payment is not returned as a new sale). This is because in salam, after iqala, the seller's admission is given weight.

Key Terms
اقالہ (iqala): Mutual rescission of a sale; both parties agree to cancel the contract.
بیع سلم (bay' salam): Forward sale: payment in advance for future delivery of a specified commodity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The judge decides based on market price.
B) Both take oaths and the original sale is restored.
C) The buyer's statement is accepted.
D) The sale is annulled without restoring the original sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, after iqala, a price dispute leads to mutual oath and reinstatement of the original sale, except in salam where the seller's word is accepted and the contract does not revert.

Women's Testimony and Seller's Oath in Defect Claims
Clarifying the interplay of women's testimony and seller's oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires the seller's oath even after women's testimony establishes a hidden defect, and apply the rule that the timing of the defect determines the buyer's right of return.

Core Idea
Women's testimony proves the defect exists; seller's oath proves when it existed.

This passage addresses a seeming contradiction: if women's testimony is sufficient for hidden defects, why does the seller still take an oath? The answer lies in the distinction between establishing the defect's existence (women's testimony) and establishing its timing (seller's oath). The buyer's right to return depends on the defect being present during the seller's ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The passage concerns a hidden defect (thayyib) in a slave-girl and the interplay of women's testimony and seller's oath.
2
State the apparent objection
Objection: If women's testimony suffices for hidden defects, why require seller's oath? Doesn't that abandon the hadith?
3
Trace the Hanafi response
Women's testimony proves the current state; seller's oath proves the defect's timing. Both are needed for the right of return.
4
Apply the rule
The buyer can only return for defects that existed during seller's possession. So if women testify to thayyib, the seller swears it wasn't thayyib when he had it; if he swears, buyer cannot return.
This resolves the contradiction: both the hadith and the oath are applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادة
اشرف الهدایہ جلد - وقع
والعيب يثبت سے ایک سوال کا جواب ہے۔
سوال یہ ہے کہ سابق میں گذر چکا کہ جن عیوب پر مرد مطلع نہ ہو سکتے ہوں ان میں عورتوں کی شہادت کافی ہے۔ لہذا عورتوں کے یہ شہادت دینے کی وجہ سے کہ مبیعہ باندی نیب ہے مشتری کو واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہونا چاہئے تھا۔ حالانکہ آپ نے بائع سے قسم لینا ضروری قرار دیا ہے۔ پس بائع سے قسم لینے کو ضروری قرار دینا حدیث شهادة النساء جائزة پر عمل کو ترک کرنا ہے۔
جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ جب عورتوں نے یہ کہا کہ مبیعہ باندی ثیبہ ہے تو مبیعہ باندی کافی الحال شبہ ہونا ثابت ہو گیا۔ پس جب مریعہ باندی کا عورتوں کی شہادت سے ثیبہ ہونا ثابت ہو گیا۔ تو حدیث پر عمل ہو گیا۔ پھر اس کے بعد بالع سے اس پر قسم کی جائے گی۔ کہ یہ باندی جس وقت میرے پاس تھی تو اس میں یہ عیب نہیں تھا یعنی اس وقت یہ ثیبہ نہیں تھی ۔ اور بائع سے اس قسم کی ضرورت اس لئے پیش آئی کہ مشتری کو اُسی عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار ہوتا ہے۔ جو عیب بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا ہو اور جو عیب بائع کے پاس نہ ہو بلکہ بعد میں پیدا ہوا ہو اس کی وجہ سے مشتری کو بیع واپس کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا۔ اس جواب کے بعد کوئی اعتراض نہ ہوگا۔
Book of Testimony. Ashraf al-Hidaya Volume - Occurrence. This is an answer to a question regarding the establishment of a defect. The question is: It has passed previously that in defects which men cannot be aware of, women's testimony is sufficient. Therefore, due to women testifying that the sold slave-girl is thayyib (deflowered), the buyer should have the right to return. Yet you (the author) have deemed it necessary that the seller take an oath. So deeming the seller's oath necessary amounts to abandoning the hadith 'The testimony of women is permissible.' The answer: When women said that the sold slave-girl is thayyib, then the slave-girl being thayyib is established in the present state. So when the slave-girl's being thayyib is established by women's testimony, the hadith is acted upon. Then after that, the seller will be asked to take an oath about this: that this slave-girl did not have this defect when she was in my possession, i.e., she was not thayyib at that time. The need for the seller's oath arose because the buyer has the right to return the sold item only due to a defect that arose while the seller possessed it, and not due to a defect that arose later. After this answer, there will be no objection.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_with_proof that resolves a seeming contradiction in the Hanafi position on defects in a slave-girl. The issue: Women's testimony is accepted for defects hidden from men (like defloration). If women testify the slave-girl is thayyib, why does the seller still take an oath? The answer: Women's testimony establishes the current state (thayyib), fulfilling the hadith. But the seller's oath determines whether the defect existed at the time of his ownership. The right of return (khiyar al-'ayb) applies only to defects present during the seller's possession. Thus both are applied: women's testimony proves the defect; seller's oath proves its timing. This is a Hanafi rule: the buyer cannot return if the defect arose after sale.

Key Terms
ثیبہ (thayyib): A non-virgin woman, a defect in a slave-girl regarding defloration.
بائع (ba'i'): The seller in the transaction.
مشتری (mushtari): The buyer in the transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) To establish that the defect existed before sale
C) To establish that the defect did not exist during his ownership, so buyer cannot return
D) To prove the slave-girl is a virgin

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, for hidden defect claims, women's testimony proves the defect's existence, while the seller's oath proves its timing; both are necessary for the right of return.

Testimony of a Usurer: Between Absolute Rejection and the Condition of Notoriety
Ruling with condition: testimony of a usurer rejected only if notorious

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a usurer and apply the condition of notoriety derived from istihsan.

Core Idea
A usurer's testimony is rejected only if notorious, avoiding undue hardship.

This passage presents a nuanced Hanafi ruling. While Al-Quduri states that a usurer's testimony is absolutely rejected, the commentator cites Al-Mabsut, adding a crucial condition: the usurer must be notorious among people. This refinement relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent excessive hardship, since many people engage in invalid contracts similar to usury. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying the law of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and its basis
Al-Quduri states that the testimony of a usurer is absolutely rejected. The proof: usury is a major sin, and the testimony of a committer of a major sin is rejected.
2
Recognize the expansion and condition
Al-Mabsut qualifies the ruling: rejection only if the usurer is notorious among people. This condition is added to avoid hardship.
This is an example of expansion (tawsi') through istihsan.
3
Analyze the istihsan reasoning
If every usurer's testimony were rejected, very few people would be acceptable witnesses, causing great difficulty. Islamic law removes hardship and difficulty.
istihsan overrides the strict analogy of the general rule.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that only a notorious usurer's testimony is rejected. This balances the prohibition of riba with the need for functional testimony.
This is the position applied in fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ سود کھانے والے کی گواہی بھی قبول نہیں کی جائے گی اور نرد اور شطرنج کے ساتھ جوا کھیلنے والے کی گواہی بھی قبول نہ ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ سود خوری اور قمار بازی دونوں کبیرہ گناہ ہیں اور سابق میں گزر چکا ہے کہ کبیرہ گناہ کے مرتکب کی گواہی قبول نہیں کی جاتی۔ لہذا ہود خور اور قمار باز کی گواہی بھی قبول نہ ہوگی ۔ لیکن مبسوط میں یہ شرط مذکور ہے کہ سود خور اگر سود خوری میں مشہور بین الناس ہو تو اس کی گواہی قبول نہ ہوگی۔ اور اگر سود خوری میں مشہور نہ ہو تو اس کی گواہی قبول کی جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ انسان بالعموم عقود فاسدہ کا مرتکب ہوتا ہے اور بہت کم لوگ ایسے ہیں جو عقود فاسدہ سے بچ جاتے ہیں اور عقود فاسدہ بھی رہا اور سود کے معنی میں ہے۔ پس اگر مطلقاً سود خوری کے ساتھ مبتلا شخص کی گواہی کو رد کر دیا گیا تو دنیا میں بہت کم لوگ مقبول الشہادت رہ جائیں گے۔ اور اس صورت میں دقت کا پیش آنا ایک بدیہی امر ہے۔ حالانکہ شریعت اسلام میں دقت اور حرج کو دور کیا گیا۔ لہذا اسی سود خور کی گواہی مردود ہوگی جو سود خوری میں مشہور ہو۔
Sahib al-Quduri said: The testimony of a person who consumes usury will not be accepted, and the testimony of a person who gambles with backgammon and chess will not be accepted. The proof is that usury and gambling are both major sins, and it has passed earlier that the testimony of the committer of a major sin is not accepted. Therefore, the testimony of a usurer and a gambler will not be accepted. However, in al-Mabsut it is stated that if the usurer is notorious among people for usury, then his testimony will not be accepted. But if he is not notorious for usury, his testimony will be accepted. Because generally a person commits invalid contracts, and very few people avoid invalid contracts, and invalid contracts are also similar to usury in meaning. So if absolutely every person involved in usury were rejected, very few people in the world would remain acceptable witnesses, and difficulty would obviously occur. While Islamic law removes difficulty and hardship. Therefore, only that usurer's testimony is rejected who is notorious for usury.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a usurer's testimony. Al-Quduri initially states it is rejected absolutely. The commentator then cites al-Mabsut, adding a condition: rejection only if the usurer is notorious. The reasoning is based on istihsan (juristic preference): avoiding hardship, since many people engage in some form of invalid contracts similar to usury. The hadith or Qur'anic prohibition of usury is not quoted here, but the proof is that it is a kabirah.

Key Terms
مشہور بین الناس (mashhur bayn al-nas): Notorious among people; the condition for rejecting a usurer's testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
