كتاب الوكالة
النكاح کے مانند ہو گا اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ عقد کرنے والا در حقیقت وکیل ہے اسلئے کہ عقد کلام کے ساتھ قائم ہوتا ہے اور اس کی عبارت کا صحیح ہونا اسکے آدمی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے اور اسی طرح حکما کیونکہ وہ عقد کو موکل کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی ہے اور اگر وکیل، مؤکل کی طرف سے سفیر ہوتا تو اس سے مستغنی نہ ہوتا جیسے اینچی اور جب ایسا ہے تو وکیل حقوق میں اصیل ہوا۔ لہذا حقوق عقد اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے۔ اسی وجہ سے کتاب میں کہا کہ وکیل ہمیشہ سپرد کرے اور ثمن پر قبضہ کرے اور جب خریدا ہو تو اس سے ثمن کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا اور مبیع پر قبضہ کرے اور عیب کے سلسلے میں وکیل سے مخاصمہ کیا جائے گا اور وکیل عیب کے سلسلے میں مخاصمہ کرے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ سب باتیں حقوق میں سے ہیں اور مؤکل کیلئے ملک وکیل کا نائب ہو کر ثابت ہوگی۔ توکیل سابق پر نظر کرتے ہوئے جیسے غلام ہبہ قبول کرے اور شکار کرنے اور لکڑیاں جمع کرے۔ یہی صحیح ہے۔ مصنف نے کہا کہ عیب کے مسئلہ میں ایک تفصیل ہے جس کو ہم انشاء اللہ ذکر کریں۔
تشریح: صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ وہ عقود جن کو وکیل لوگ منعقد کرتے ہیں ان کی دو قسمیں ہیں۔
ا۔ ایک وہ عقد جس کے حقوق وکیل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں۔
-۲ دوم وہ عقد جس کے حقوق مؤکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں۔ پہلی قسم کے لئے ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ عقد جس کو وکیل اپنی طرف منسوب کرے اور اس کا منسوب کرنا صحیح ہو اور موکل کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے بے نیاز ہو جیسے بیع و اجارہ تو اس عقد کے حقوق وکیل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے اور موکل کے ساتھ متعلق نہ ہوں گے۔ یعنی اس عقد کے حقوق کی ذمہ داری وکیل پر عائد ہوگی نہ کہ موکل پر۔ مثلا وکیل نے کہا کہ میں نے تیرے ہاتھ پر یہ چیز فروخت کی تو مبیع سپرد کرنا وکیل کے ذمہ ہے۔ علامہ ابن الہمام نے اس ضابطہ پر نقض وارد کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ وکیل اگر عبد مجور علیہ ہو تو ان دونوں کے عقد کے حقوق مؤکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں اگر چہ عقد کو وکیل اپنی طرف منسوب کرتا ہے جیسا مسئلہ میں گذرا۔
حضرت امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ عقد کے حقوق مؤکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں یعنی حقوق عقد کی ذمہ داری مؤکل پر عائد ہوتی ہے اور اسی قول کے امام مالک اور امام احمد ہیں۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حقوق عقد محکم تصرف یعنی ملکیت کے تابع ہیں اور حکم یعنی ملکیت موکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتی ہے۔ یعنی اگر تصرف عقد وکیل بالبیع نے کیا تو مؤکل ہی ثمن کا مالک ہوگا اور اگر وکیل بالشراء نے تصرف عقد کیا تو موکل ہی مبیع کا مالک ہوگا۔ بہر حال ملکیت موکل کیلئے ثابت ہوتی ہے اور جب ملکیت موکل کے لئے ثابت ہوتی ہے تو اس کے توابع یعنی حقوق عقد بھی مؤکل کیلئے ثابت ہوں گے اور مؤکل ہی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے اور اس قسم میں وکیل، اینچی اور وکیل بالنکاح کے مانند ہو گیا اور اینچی اور وکیل بالنکاح بالاتفاق حقوق عقد کے ذمہ دار نہیں ہوتے۔ یعنی حقوق نہ اینچی کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں اور نہ وکیل بالنکاح کی طرف لوٹتے ہیں۔ پس اسی طرح اس قسم میں بھی حقوق عقد وکیل کی طرف عود نہ کریں گے بلکہ مؤکل کی طرف عود کریں گے۔ اینچی کی مثال: خالد نے حامد کو شاہد کے پاس بھیجا۔ حامد نے پیغام پہنچایا کہ خالد نے پیغام دیا ہے کہ میں نے تیرا گھوڑا ایک سو روپیہ میں خریدا اور شاہد نے منظور کیا تو اینچی یعنی حامد کے ساتھ کوئی حق متعلق نہ ہو گا یعنی اس کی کچھ ذمہ داری نہیں ہے۔ اسی طرح وکیل بالنکاح ذمہ دار نہیں ہوتا ہے اور اسی طرح خرید و فروخت کا وکیل بھی ذمہ دار نہیں ہے بلکہ موکل ذمہ دار ہے۔
ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس قسم میں عقد کرنے والا حقیقتا بھی اور حکما بھی وکیل ہے۔ وکیل حقیقتا تو اسلئے کہ عاقد ہے کہ عقد کلام کے ساتھ قائم ہوتا ہے اور کلام کرنے والا وکیل ہے نہ کہ موکل، لہذا عقد کرنے والا وکیل ہو نہ کہ موکل اور وکیل کی عبارت یعنی وکیل کا کلام صحیح بھی ہے۔ مگر وکیل کے کلام کا صحیح ہونا دلیل ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ ایک عاقل آدمی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ یعنی عاقل آدمی ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کیلئے ایجاب و قبول کرنے کی اہلیت بھی موجود ہے تو یہ عقد وکیل ہی کی طرف سے واقع ہوگا اور وکیل ہی عاقد ہوگا اور اس قسم میں وکیل حکماً عاقد اسلئے ہے کہ وکیل عقد کو مؤکل کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی ہے۔ اگر وکیل مؤکل کی طرف سے محض سفیر اور قاصد ہوتا تو وہ عقد کو موکل کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہ ہوتا۔ جیسے اینچی مرسل کی طرف عقد منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہیں ہے اور وکیل بالنکاح مؤکل کی طرف عقد نکاح منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہیں ہے اور جب ایسا ہے یعنی وکیل اس قسم میں حقیقتا بھی عاقد ہے اور حکما بھی عاقد ہے تو وہ حقوق عقد کے سلسلے میں اصیل ہوگا اور جب وکیل حقوق عقد کے سلسلہ میں اصیل ہے تو حقوق عقد اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے اور اسی کی طرف عود کریں گے۔ وکیل حقوق کے سلسلہ میں چونکہ اصیل ہوتا ہے اسی لئے صاحب قدوری نے اپنی مختصر القدوری میں یا امام محمد نے جامع صغیر یا مبسوط میں فرمایا کہ اگر وکیل بالبیع ہے تو وہ مبیع مشتری کے حوالہ کرے گا اور ثمن پر قبضہ کرے گا اور اگر وکیل بالشراء ہے تو اس سے ثمن کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا اور وہ خود مبیع پر قبضہ کرے گا۔ اور اگر وکیل بالبیع ہے اور مشتری مبیع کے کسی عیب پر مطلع ہو گیا تو مشتری اس عیب کے سلسلہ میں وکیل ہی سے گفتگو کرے گا اور اگر وکیل بالشراء ہے اور مبیع کے کسی عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو اس عیب کے سلسلہ میں وکیل ہی بائع سے گفتگو کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا نہ کہ موکل۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ تمام چیزیں حقوق عقد میں سے ہیں اور جملہ حقوق عقد وکیل کی طرف عود کرتے ہیں۔ لہذا مذکورہ تمام چیزیں وکیل کی طرف عود کریں گی اور وکیل ہی ان کا ذمہ دار ہوگا۔
امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب: والملك يثبت للمؤكل خلافة عنه ... الخ سے امام شافعی کی دلیل (کہ حقوق محکم تصرف یعنی ملکیت کے تابع ہیں اور حکم یعنی ملکیت موکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتی ہے، لہذا اس کے توابع بھی مؤکل ہی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے) کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ توکیل سابق کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے مؤکل کیلئے ملک اگر چہ ابتداء ثابت ہوتی ہے لیکن اصالۃ نہیں۔ وکیل کی نیابت سے مؤکل کیلئے ملک ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ وکیل موکل کا نائب ہے تصرف کا استفادہ کرنے میں اور مؤکل وکیل کا نائب ہے ملک ثابت ہونے کے حق میں۔ بہر حال ملکیت موکل کیلئے ابتداء ثابت ہو جاتی ہے اگر چہ اصالۃ نہیں ہوتی بلکہ نیابتاً ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ پس جب مؤکل کیلئے اصالتاً ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی تو ملک کے تابع ہو کر حقوق عقد بھی مؤکل کیلئے ثابت نہ ہوں گے اور جب مؤکل کیلئے حقوق ثابت نہیں ہوتے تو وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوں گے۔ ثبوت ملک کے حق میں مؤکل کا وکیل کی طرف سے نائب ہونا ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ غلام ہبہ قبول کرتا ہے اور شکار مارتا ہے اور لکڑی جمع کرتا ہے۔ یعنی شئے موہوبہ، شکار اور لکڑیوں کا مالک ابتداء مولیٰ ہی ہوتا ہے لیکن اصالۃ نہیں بلکہ غلام کی نیابت میں مولی کو ملکیت حاصل ہوتی ہے کیونکہ اس سبب سے مالک ہونے میں مولی غلام کا قائم مقام ہے۔ اسی طرح وکیل کی نیابت میں موکل کیلئے۔
Book of Agency. It will be like marriage, and our proof is that the one who makes the contract is in reality the agent, because the contract is established through speech, and the correctness of his expression is due to his being human, and similarly in legal consideration because he is independent of attributing the contract to the principal. If the agent were merely a messenger from the principal, he would not be independent, like a messenger. When this is the case, the agent is the principal in matters of rights. Therefore, the rights of the contract will attach to him. For this reason, it is said in the book that the agent must deliver the goods, take possession of the price, and when he buys, the price will be demanded from him, and he takes possession of the sold item, and regarding defects, the agent will be addressed, and the agent will address regarding defects. Because all these matters are among the rights, and ownership for the principal is established through the agency of the agent, considering the prior authorization, like a slave who accepts a gift, hunts, and collects firewood. This is correct. The author said that there is a detail in the issue of defects which we will mention, God willing. Explanation: The author of al-Quduri said that the contracts which agents conclude are of two types: (1) A contract whose rights attach to the agent. (2) A contract whose rights attach to the principal. For the first type, the rule is: every contract which the agent attributes to himself and his attribution is correct, and he is independent of attributing to the principal, like sale and lease, then the rights of that contract attach to the agent and not to the principal. That is, the responsibility for the rights of that contract lies on the agent, not on the principal. For example, if the agent said, 'I sold this thing to you,' then delivering the sold item is the agent's responsibility. Ibn al-Humam criticized this rule, saying: if the agent is a hired laborer or a rented slave, then the rights of their contract attach to the principal, even if the agent attributes the contract to himself, as in the earlier issue. Imam al-Shafi'i said that the rights of the contract attach to the principal, meaning the responsibility for the contract rights lies on the principal, and Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad hold this view. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that the rights of the contract follow the ruling of the transaction, i.e., ownership, and the ruling (ownership) attaches to the principal. That is, if the agent making a sale performs the contract, the principal is the owner of the price, and if the agent making a purchase performs the contract, the principal is the owner of the purchased item. In any case, ownership is established for the principal, and when ownership is established for the principal, its attachments, i.e., the rights of the contract, are also established for the principal and attach to him. In this type, the agent becomes like a messenger and an agent for marriage. The messenger and the agent for marriage, by consensus, are not responsible for the rights of the contract, i.e., the rights do not return to the messenger nor to the marriage agent. So similarly in this type, the rights of the contract will not return to the agent but to the principal. Example of a messenger: Khalid sent Hamid to Shahid; Hamid delivered the message that Khalid has sent word saying, 'I have bought your horse for one hundred rupees,' and Shahid accepted. Then no right attaches to the messenger (i.e., Hamid has no responsibility). Similarly, the marriage agent is not responsible, and likewise the agent for sale and purchase is not responsible; rather the principal is responsible. Our proof is that in this type, the one who makes the contract is both actually and legally the agent. Actually the agent because he is the contractor, for the contract is established through speech, and the one who speaks is the agent, not the principal. Therefore, the contractor is the agent, not the principal. The agent's expression, i.e., the agent's speech, is valid—but its validity is not because of being evidence, but because he is a rational human being. That is, due to being rational, he has the capacity to make offer and acceptance, so the contract occurs on his behalf and he is the contractor. And in this type, the agent is legally the contractor because he is independent of attributing the contract to the principal. If the agent were merely a messenger and envoy from the principal, he would not be independent of attributing the contract to the principal, like a messenger who is not independent of attributing the contract to the sender, and the marriage agent is not independent of attributing the marriage contract to the principal. When this is the case—that the agent is both actually and legally the contractor—then he is a principal (asl) regarding the rights of the contract. And when the agent is the principal regarding the rights, then the rights attach to him and return to him. Since the agent is the principal regarding rights, therefore the author of al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar, or Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir or al-Mabsut, said: if the agent is for sale, he will deliver the sold item to the buyer and take possession of the price; if the agent is for purchase, the price will be demanded from him and he himself will take possession of the purchased item. And if the agent is for sale and the buyer discovers a defect in the sold item, the buyer will discuss the defect with the agent. If the agent is for purchase and discovers a defect in the purchased item, the agent is authorized to discuss the defect with the seller, not the principal. The proof is that all the mentioned things are among the rights of the contract, and all rights of the contract return to the agent. Therefore, all the mentioned things will return to the agent, and he will be responsible for them. The answer to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: 'And ownership is established for the principal as his deputy...' etc. The answer to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof (that rights follow the ruling of the transaction i.e. ownership, and the ruling i.e. ownership attaches to the principal, so its attachments also attach to the principal) is that by considering the prior authorization, ownership for the principal is established initially but not as a principal. Ownership is established for the principal through the agency of the agent. In summary, the agent is the deputy of the principal in benefiting from the transaction, and the principal is the deputy of the agent in the establishment of ownership. Nevertheless, ownership is established initially for the principal, though not as a principal but as a deputy. So when ownership is not established for the principal as a principal, the rights of the contract as attachments of ownership are not established for the principal either. And when rights are not established for the principal, they will be established for the agent. In the establishment of ownership, the principal's being the deputy of the agent is like when a slave accepts a gift, hunts, and gathers firewood. That is, the owner of the gifted object, the hunted game, and the firewood is initially the master, but not as a principal; rather, the master acquires ownership through the slave's agency, because in the cause of ownership the master stands in the place of the slave. Similarly, for the principal through the agent's agency.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a detailed inter-madhhab dispute concerning the attachment of contract rights in agency (wakalah). The author, a Hanafi commentator, explains the position of the Hanafi school against the Shafi'i school (and also Malik and Ahmad) regarding to whom the rights and obligations of a contract concluded by an agent belong.

The passage opens by stating the Hanafi position: the agent (wakil) is the true contractor (asil) in the contract. Therefore, all rights of the contract, such as delivering the subject matter, receiving payment, and dealing with defects, attach to the agent, not to the principal (muwakkil). This is because the contract is established through the speech of the agent, not the principal. The agent is a rational person capable of making offers and acceptances. Additionally, the agent is legally independent; he does not need to attribute the contract to the principal (unlike a messenger who must attribute the contract to the sender). Thus, the agent is the principal in rights, and all related responsibilities fall on him.

The author then cites the matn (likely from al-Quduri) which divides contracts concluded by agents into two types: (1) contracts where rights attach to the agent (the type under discussion), and (2) contracts where rights attach to the principal. For the first type, the rule is that if the agent can correctly attribute the contract to himself and is independent of attributing to the principal (like sale and lease), then rights attach to the agent. The author notes a criticism by Ibn al-Humam regarding a hired laborer or a rented slave, but this does not affect the general principle.

Next, the opposing view of Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik and Ahmad) is presented: they argue that the rights of the contract should attach to the principal because ownership belongs to the principal. Since the rights follow ownership, they should return to the principal. They analogize the agent to a mere messenger (rasul) or a marriage agent (wakil bi al-nikah), who by consensus are not responsible for the contract's rights. The example of a messenger is given: a messenger who simply conveys an offer and acceptance has no personal liability.

The Hanafi school then provides its counter-argument. The agent is not merely a messenger; he is the actual contractor (haqiqatan) because he performs the offer and acceptance. Furthermore, he is legally (hukman) the contractor because he does not need to attribute the contract to the principal (unlike a marriage agent who must). Therefore, the agent is the principal regarding rights, and the rights attach to him. The response to Shafi'i's ownership argument is that ownership is established for the principal indirectly through the agent's agency, not directly as a principal. Hence, the rights, being dependent on ownership, are not directly on the principal but rather on the agent, who is the original source. The author illustrates this with an example: a slave who accepts a gift, hunts, or collects firewood—ownership of those items goes to the master, but it is established through the agency of the slave. The master is the deputy of the slave in the cause of ownership. Similarly, in agency, the principal obtains ownership through the agent's actions, but the rights of the contract remain with the agent.

Finally, the passage confirms that the authoritative Hanafi position (mufta bihi) is that in these contracts (sale, purchase), the agent bears all rights and responsibilities, including delivery, receipt of price, and defect claims. This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position as recorded in the works of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakīl): An agent who acts on behalf of another (al-muwakkil) in concluding contracts or performing legal acts.
اصیل (aṣīl): Principal: the one who is directly responsible for the rights and obligations arising from a contract, as opposed to a mere representative.
اینچی (īnchī): A messenger or intermediary who simply conveys an offer and acceptance without exercising independent judgment or contract formation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in a contract of sale concluded by an agent, who is responsible for delivering the sold item and handling defect claims?
A) The principal
B) The agent
C) Both jointly
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the agent is the principal in contract rights; ownership passes to the principal indirectly, but all immediate rights and liabilities remain with the agent.

Who Bears Contractual Rights in Agency: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi vs Shafi'i dispute on agent's contractual rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that contractual rights belong to the agent in a sale/purchase agency, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The agent is the actual contracting party; all contractual rights belong to him, not the principal.

This passage tackles a fundamental disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: who bears the rights and obligations when an agent buys or sells? The answer determines who must deliver the item, who collects the price, and who handles defects. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school weighs the act of contracting versus the underlying ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that the agent (wakil) is the actual contracting party and bears all contractual rights (delivery, price, defects).
The Hanafi position is based on the agent being the speaker of the contract.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that contractual rights follow ownership and thus belong to the principal (muwakkil).
The Shafi'i view treats rights as accessories of ownership.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the Hanafi evidence: the agent speaks the contract, is independent of attribution, and ownership vests in the principal only through deputyship. The hadith about ownership of close relatives supports that ownership does not vest in the agent.
The proof includes both rational (haqiqatan and hukman) and scriptural (hadith) elements.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Shafi'i
Understand how the Hanafis rebut the Shafi'i claim: ownership is established for the principal, but not as a principal; it is through the agent's deputyship. Therefore, accessories (rights) do not follow ownership to the principal.
The agent's dual role (speaker and deputy) allows splitting ownership (to principal) and rights (to agent).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: the buyer must deal with the agent for defects, delivery, and price, not the principal.
This affects litigation and day-to-day transactions.

طرح خرید و فروخت کا وکیل بھی ذمہ دار نہیں ہے بلکہ موکل ذمہ دار ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ اس قسم میں عقد کرنے والا حقیقتا بھی اور حکم وکیل ہے وکیل حقیقتا تو اسلئے کہ عاقد ہے کہ عقد کلام کے ساتھ قائم ہوتا ہے اور کلام کرنے والا وکیل ہے نہ کہ متوکل لہذا عقد کرنے والا وکیل ہو نہ کہ موکل اور وکیل کی عبارت یعنی وکیل کا کلام صحیح بھی ہے۔ مگر وکیل کے کلام کا صحیح ہونا دلیل ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ ایک عاقل آدمی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ یعنی عاقل آدمی ہونے کیوجہ سے اس کیلئے ایجاب و قبول کرنے کی اہلیت بھی موجود ہے تو یہ عقد وکیل ہی کی طرف سے واقع ہوگا اور وکیل ہی عاقد ہوگا اور اس قسم میں وکیل حکماً عاقد اسلئے ہے کہ وکیل عقد کو مؤکل کی طرف منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی ہے اگر وکیل مؤکل کی طرف سے محض سفیر اور قاصد ہوتا تو وہ عقد کو موکل کی طرف سے منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہ ہوتا ۔ جیسے [؟] ، مرسل کی طرف عقد منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہیں ہے اور وکیل بالنکاح مؤکل کی طرف عقد نکاح منسوب کرنے سے مستغنی نہیں ہے اور جب ایسا ہے یعنی وکیل اس قسم میں حقیقتا بھی عاقد ہے اور حکما بھی عاقد ہے تو وہ حقوق عقد کے سلسلے میں اصیل ہوگا اور جب وکیل حقوق عقد کے سلسلہ میں اصیل ہے تو حقوق عقد اس کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے اور اسی کی طرف عود کریں گے وکیل حقوق کے سلسلہ میں چونکہ اصیل ہوتا ہے اسی لئے صاحب قدوری نے اپنی مختصر القدوری میں یا امام محمد نے جامع صغیر یا مبسوط میں فرمایا کہ اگر وکیل بالبیع ہے تو وہ مبیع مشتری کے حوالہ کرے گا اور ثمن پر قبضہ کرے گا اور وکیل بالشراء ہے تو اس سے ثمن کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا اور وہ خود مبیع پر قبضہ کرے گا۔ اور اگر وکیل بالبیع سے اور مشتری بیع کے کسی عیب پر مطلع ہو گیا تو مشتری اس عیب کے سلسلہ میں وکیل ہی سے گفتگو کرے گا اور اگر وکیل بالشراء ہے اور مبیع کے کسی عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو اس عیب کے سلسلہ میں وکیل ہی سے گفتگو کرے گا اور اگر وکیل بالشراء ہے اور مبیع کے کسی عیب پر مطلع ہوا تو اس عیب کے سلسلہ میں وکیل ہی بائع سے گفتگو کرنے کا مجاز ہوگا نہ کہ موکل۔ دلیل

یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ تمام چیزیں حقوق عقد میں سے ہیں۔ اور جملہ حقوق عقد وکیل کی طرف عود کرتے ہیں۔ لہذا مذکورہ تمام چیزیں وکیل کی طرف عود کر یں گی اور وکیل ہی ان کا ذمہ دار ہوگا۔

امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب .. والملك يثبت للمؤكل خلافة عنه ... الخ سے امام شافعی کی دلیل کہ حقوق حکم تصرف عنه یعنی ملکیت کے تابع ہیں اور حکم یعنی ملکیت موکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتی ہے۔ لہذا اس کے توابع بھی مؤکل ہی کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے ) کا جواب ہے۔ جواب کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ توکیل سابق کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے مؤکل کیلئے ملک اگر چہ ابتداء ثابت ہوتی ہے لیکن اصالی نہیں۔ وکیل کی نیابت سے مؤکل کیلئے ملک ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ خلاصہ یہ کہ وکیل موکل کا نائب ہے تصرف کا استفادہ کرنے میں اور مؤکل وکیل کا نائب ہے۔ ملک ثابت ہونے کے حق میں بہر حال ملکیت موکل کیلئے ابتداء ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ اگر چہ اصالہ نہیں ہوتی بلکہ نیابتہ ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ پس جب مؤکل کیلئے اصالتہ ملک ثابت نہیں ہوتی تو ملک کے تابع ہو کر حقوق عقد بھی مؤکل کیلئے ثابت نہ ہوں گے اور جب مؤکل کیلئے حقوق ثابت نہیں ہوتے تو وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوں گے۔

ثبوت ملک کے حق میں مؤکل کا وکیل کی طرف سے نائب ہونا ایسا ہی ہے جیسا کہ غلام ہبہ قبول کرتا ہے۔ اور شکار مارتا ہے اور لکڑی جمع کرتا ہے۔ یعنی شی، موہو بہ شکار اور لکڑیوں کا مالک ابتداء مولیٰ ہی ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن اصالی نہیں ۔ بلکہ غلام کی نیابت میں مولی کو ملکیت حاصل ہوتی ہے کیونکہ اس سبب سے مالک ہونے میں مولی غلام کا قائم مقام ہے۔ اسی طرح وکیل کی نیابت میں موکل کیلئے۔

صاحب ہدایہ نے کہا کہ موکل کیلئے ملک ثابت کرنے کا یہ طریقہ ابو طاہر دباس کا طریقہ ہے اس کو اکثر احناف نے پسند کیا ہے۔ شمس الائمہ سرخسی نے کہا کہ طریقہ اصح ہے اور مصنف ہدایہ نے بھی فرمایا کہ یہی صحیح ہے لیکن شیخ ابو الحسن کرخی نے فرمایا کہ خریدنے کی وجہ سے ملک تو ابتداء وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوتی ہے لیکن پھر مؤکل کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتی ہے۔ پس جب امام کرخی کے نزدیک ملک ابتداء وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوتی ہے تو ملک کے تابع ہو کر حقوق عقد بھی وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوں گے۔ اور مؤکل کیلئے ثابت نہ ہوں گے۔ حاصل یہ کہ شیخ ابوطاہر دباس کے نزدیک وکیل کے کسی چیز کو خریدنے کی وجہ سے ملکیت ابتداء ہی مؤکل کیلئے ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ لیکن اصالہ نہیں بلکہ وکیل کی نیابت میں اور امام کرخی کے نزدیک ملک ابتداء تو وکیل کیلئے ثابت ہوتی ہے لیکن پھر موکل کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتی ہے۔

ابوطاہر دباس کے قول کے صحیح ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر وکیل نے اپنی منکوحہ کو خریدا جو دوسرے کی باندی ہے یا اپنے کسی ذی رحم محرم کو خریدا جو دوسرے کا غلام ہے تو نہ یہ نکاح فاسد ہوتا ہے اور نہ وہ ذی رحم محرم اس پر آزاد ہوتا۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ مشتری یعنی وکیل کے لئے ملکیت ثابت نہیں بلکہ ابتداء ہی مؤکل کے لئے ملکیت ثابت ہوتی ہے اگر چہ وکیل کی نیابت میں ثابت ہوتی ہے کیونکہ اگر وکیل کیلئے ملک ثابت ہو جاتی تو پہلی صورت میں اس کا نکاح فاسد ہو جاتا۔ اسلئے کہ ملک یمین سے ملک نکاح فاسد ہو جاتا ہے اور دوسری صورت میں خرید کردہ غلام آزاد ہو جاتا کیونکہ رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے: من ملک ذا رحم محرم عتق عليه

صاحب فتح القدیر اور صاحب عنایہ نے امام شافعی کی دلیل کا جواب اور وکیل کیلئے حقوق عقد ثابت کرنے کیلئے اس طرح دلیل بیان کی ہے کہ وکیل کے تصرف کی دو جہتیں ہیں۔
- وکیل کے کلام سے تصرف کا حاصل ہونا۔ -۲ تصرف میں موکل کی طرف سے وکیل کا نائب ہونا۔
اب اگر ہم نے جہت اولی کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے ملک اور حقوق دونوں وکیل کیلئے ثابت کر دی جیسا کہ قیاس کا تقاضہ ہے تو مؤکل کا اس کو وکیل کرنا باطل ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں وکیل، وکیل نہیں رہے گا بلکہ اصل ہو جائے گا۔ حالانکہ وکیل تصرف میں موکل کا نائب ہے اور اصیل نہیں ہے اور اگر ملک اور حقوق دونوں موکل کیلئے ثابت کر دیں تو وکیل کا عقد کیلئے کلام کرنا باطل ہو جائے گا۔ حالانکہ عقد وکیل ہی کے کلام سے حاصل ہوا ہے۔ پس دونوں جہتوں کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے ہم نے ملک تو مؤکل کیلئے ثابت کی ہے۔ کیونکہ وکیل بنانے سے ملکیت حاصل کرنا ہی مقصود ہوتا ہے۔
اس کی طرف مصنف ہدایہ نے اعتبارا للتوكيل السابق سے اشارہ کیا ہے اور حقوق وکیل کیلئے ثابت کہتے ہیں ۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا عقد کی اس قسم اول میں حقوق وکیل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں۔ اور موکل کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہوتے۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ مسئلہ عیب میں قدرے تفصیل ہے جس کو ہم انشاء اللہ باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء كے تحت بيان کریں گے۔
فانتظروا انى معكم من المنتظرين
Book of Agency: The agent in sale and purchase is also not liable; rather, the principal is liable. Our proof is that in this type, the one contracting is both in reality and in effect the agent. In reality because he is the contracting party, since the contract is established by speech, and the speaker is the agent, not the principal. Hence, the contracting party is the agent, not the principal. And the expression of the agent, i.e., the agent's speech, is valid. However, the validity of the agent's speech is not because he is a proof, but because he is a rational person. That is, because he is a rational person, he is capable of executing offer and acceptance. So this contract will be attributed to the agent, and the agent is the contracting party. And in this type, the agent is the contracting party in effect because the agent is independent of attributing the contract to the principal. If the agent were merely a messenger and envoy from the principal, he would not be independent of attributing the contract to the principal, just as the [?] is not independent of attributing the contract to the sender, and the marriage agent is not independent of attributing the marriage contract to the principal. And since this is the case—that the agent is both in reality and in effect the contracting party—he will be the principal with respect to the rights of the contract. And when the agent is the principal in the matter of contractual rights, the rights will pertain to him and revert to him. Since the agent is the principal in matters of rights, therefore the author of al-Quduri in his Mukhtasar al-Quduri, or Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir or al-Mabsut, said: If he is an agent for sale, he will hand over the sold item to the buyer and take possession of the price; and if he is an agent for purchase, the price will be demanded from him and he himself will take possession of the sold item. And if the agent for sale and the buyer become aware of a defect in the sale, the buyer will speak about the defect only with the agent; and if the agent for purchase becomes aware of a defect in the sold item, he will speak about it to the seller, not the principal. The proof is that all these matters are among the rights of the contract, and all rights of the contract revert to the agent. Therefore, all mentioned matters will revert to the agent, and he will be responsible for them. The response to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: 'And ownership is established for the principal as a succession from him...' etc. This is the response to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof that rights follow the ruling (ownership), i.e., that ownership pertains to the principal, so its accessories also pertain to the principal. The gist of the response is that considering the prior authorization, ownership for the principal is initially established but not as a principal; it is established through the agency of the agent. In summary, the agent is the deputy of the principal in utilizing the transaction, and the principal is the deputy of the agent in the right of ownership being established. Nevertheless, ownership is initially established for the principal, although not as a principal but as a deputy. So when ownership is not established for the principal as a principal, the contractual rights as accessories of ownership will not be established for the principal; and when rights are not established for the principal, they will be established for the agent. The principal's being a deputy on behalf of the agent in the matter of establishing ownership is like a slave accepting a gift, hunting game, and collecting wood. That is, the master is initially the owner of the gifted item, game, and wood, but not as a principal; rather, the master acquires ownership through the slave's deputyship, because in being an owner through this cause, the master stands in place of the slave. Similarly, for the principal through the agent's deputyship. The author of Hidayah said that this method of establishing ownership for the principal is the method of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, and most Hanafis have approved it. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi said that it is the most correct method, and the author of Hidayah also said that this is correct. But Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said that by purchase, ownership is initially established for the agent, then it transfers to the principal. So according to Imam al-Karkhi, since ownership is initially established for the agent, contractual rights as accessories of ownership will also be established for the agent, not for the principal. In summary, according to Shaykh Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, by the agent's purchase, ownership is initially established for the principal, but not as a principal, rather through the agent's deputyship. According to Imam al-Karkhi, ownership is initially established for the agent, then it transfers to the principal. The proof of the correctness of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas's statement is that if the agent purchases his own married wife who is another's slave-girl, or purchases one of his own close relatives who is another's slave, then neither does the marriage become invalid, nor does that close relative become free. This shows that ownership is not established for the buyer (i.e., the agent); rather, ownership is initially established for the principal, even if through the agent's deputyship. Because if ownership were established for the agent, then in the first case his marriage would become invalid (since ownership by a slave-owner invalidates marriage), and in the second case the purchased slave would become free (since the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever owns a close relative, that relative becomes free'). The authors of Fath al-Qadir and al-'Inayah responded to Imam al-Shafi'i's proof and presented proof for establishing contractual rights for the agent in this way: The agent's transaction has two aspects: (1) the transaction being effected by the agent's speech, and (2) the agent being the deputy of the principal in the transaction. Now if we consider the first aspect and establish both ownership and rights for the agent (as qiyas requires), then the principal's appointment of him as agent becomes void, because in that case the agent would not remain an agent but would become a principal, whereas the agent is a deputy, not a principal. And if we establish both ownership and rights for the principal, then the agent's speech for the contract becomes void, whereas the contract is effected by the agent's speech. Therefore, considering both aspects, we establish ownership for the principal (since obtaining ownership is the purpose of appointing an agent—the author of Hidayah pointed to this by 'considering the prior authorization') and we establish rights for the agent. So it is established that in this first type of contract, rights pertain to the agent, not to the principal. The author of Hidayah said that there is some detail in the matter of defects, which we will explain, God willing, under the chapter of agency for sale and purchase. So wait, for I am with you among those who wait.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses a fundamental issue in the law of agency (wakalah): who bears the rights and obligations arising from a contract of sale or purchase—the agent (wakil) or the principal (muwakkil)? The Hanafi position, as presented here, is that the agent is the actual contracting party, both in reality (haqiqatan) and in effect (hukman). Therefore, all contractual rights and obligations—such as delivery of the sold item, receipt of the price, and handling of defects—pertain to the agent, not the principal. The text begins by stating this position and then provides proofs. It argues that the contract is established by speech, and the speaker is the agent; hence the agent is the contracting party (aqid). The agent's capacity to contract is based on his rationality, not on being a proof (i.e., not because he represents the principal). Moreover, the agent is independent of attributing the contract to the principal; unlike a mere messenger (rasul) who must attribute the contract to the sender, or a marriage agent who must attribute the marriage to the groom, the general agent in sale/purchase acts in his own name. Therefore, the agent is the principal (asil) with respect to contractual rights. The text then cites the authoritative Hanafi sources: al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, and Imam Muhammad's works, which state that the agent for sale delivers the item and takes the price, and the agent for purchase is asked for the price and takes the item. Defects are to be raised with the agent, not the principal. This is because all these are rights of the contract (huquq al-aqd) which revert to the agent. Next, the text addresses the Shafi'i counter-argument. The Shafi'i school holds that ownership (milk) belongs to the principal, and since contractual rights follow ownership (as accessories of the owned property), those rights should also belong to the principal. The Hanafi response is nuanced: they accept that ownership is established for the principal initially (ibtida'an) but not as a principal (asalan); rather, it is through the agency of the agent. In the matter of ownership, the principal is the deputy of the agent, like a slave who acquires property for his master. The master owns the property initially but through the slave's deputyship. Similarly, the principal owns through the agent's deputyship. Since ownership is not established for the principal as a principal (asalah), its accessories (rights) are not established for the principal; they are established for the agent. The text then mentions two Hanafi sub-views on how ownership is established. The first, attributed to Abu Tahir al-Dabbas and preferred by most Hanafis including al-Sarakhsi and the author of Hidayah, states that ownership is initially established for the principal through the agent's deputyship (niyabah). The second, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, states that ownership is initially established for the agent and then transfers to the principal. The text provides a proof for al-Dabbas's view using practical examples: if an agent purchases his own wife who is another's slave, the marriage is not invalidated (as it would be if the agent owned the slave, because ownership by a slave-owner invalidates marriage). Similarly, if he purchases a close relative who is another's slave, that relative does not become free (as would happen if the agent owned the relative, based on the hadith 'Whoever owns a close relative, that relative becomes free'). This shows that ownership does not vest in the agent at any point; it goes directly to the principal. The text then explains that the agent's transaction has two aspects: (1) the contract being effected by the agent's speech, and (2) the agent being the principal's deputy. If we give both ownership and rights to the agent based on (1), the agency becomes meaningless (the agent becomes a principal). If we give both to the principal based on (2), the agent's speech becomes irrelevant. The compromise is to give ownership to the principal (the goal of agency) and give contractual rights to the agent (since he is the one who contracted). The author of Hidayah signals this by saying 'considering the prior authorization' (i'tibaran li al-tawkili al-sabiq). The passage concludes by noting that there is more detail on the issue of defects that will be discussed in the chapter on agency for sale and purchase.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakīl): Agent; a person appointed by the principal (muwakkil) to perform a legal act on his behalf.
موکل (muwakkil): Principal; the person who appoints an agent.
حقوق عقد (ḥuqūq al-ʿaqd): Rights and obligations arising from a contract, such as delivery, price, defects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if an agent for purchase buys a defective item, who is responsible for returning it to the seller and claiming compensation?
A) The principal (muwakkil) must handle it.
B) The agent (wakil) must handle it.
C) Both the agent and principal are jointly responsible.
D) Neither; the contract is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the agent is the actual contracting party, so all contractual rights—delivery, price, defects—belong to the agent, not the principal.

Dispute on Ownership in Agency Purchase
Presents intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute on who owns purchased property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on ownership in agency purchase, explain the proof from the hadith on emancipation, and apply the two-aspect analysis to determine rights.

Core Idea
Ownership in agency purchase belongs to the principal from the start, not the agent.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, Imam Al-Marghinani presents a key legal dispute within the Hanafi school about who owns property purchased by an agent. The majority Hanafi position, preferred by Abu Tahir al-Dabbas and upheld by later scholars, holds that ownership vests directly in the principal. We will examine the proof from a Prophetic hadith and the nuanced response to opponent views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
State the views of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas and Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi on who owns the property initially when an agent purchases on behalf of a principal.
Abu Tahir: ownership for principal ab initio. Al-Karkhi: ownership first for agent, then transfers.
2
Examine the proof from hadith
Analyze the scenario: if the agent purchases his own wife (a slave of another) or a close blood relative. If the agent owned them, marriage would invalidate and relative would become free. Since neither occurs, the agent never owned them.
The hadith 'Man malaka dha rahim mahram utiqa 'alayhi' (Whoever possesses a close blood relative, that relative becomes free) is used as proof.
3
Analyze the response to al-Shafi'i
The authors of Fath al-Qadir and Al-Inayah respond to Imam al-Shafi'i's objection by noting that the agent's act has two dimensions: his speech effectuates the contract, and he acts as deputy. Giving both ownership and rights to the principal would ignore his speech; giving both to the agent would void the agency.
Thus, ownership goes to the principal (purpose of agency) and subsidiary rights (like khiyar) go to the agent.
4
Apply the two-aspect analysis
Consider a concrete case: the agent buys a car. According to the preferred view, ownership is with the principal immediately, but the agent may have the option to return the car for defects (khiyar al-ayb).
This demonstrates the practical application of splitting ownership and rights.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference between the two views: under Abu Tahir's view, the agent cannot free a relative slave purchased because he never owns him; under al-Karkhi's view, the agent could free him before transferring ownership.
The preferred view is mufta bihi.

ابوطاہر دباس کے قول کے صحیح ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر وکیل نے اپنی منکوحہ کو خریدا جو دوسرے کی باندی ہے یا اپنے کسی ذی رحم محرم کو خریدا جو دوسرے کا غلام ہے تو نہ یہ نکاح فاسد ہوتا ہے اور نہ وہ ذی رحم محرم اس پر آزاد ہوتا۔ اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ مشتری یعنی وکیل کے لئے ملکیت ثابت نہیں بلکہ ابتداء ہی مؤکل کے لئے ملکیت ثابت ہوتی ہے اگر چہ وکیل کی نیابت میں ثابت ہوتی ہے کیونکہ اگر وکیل کیلئے ملک ثابت ہو جاتی تو پہلی صورت میں اس کا نکاح فاسد ہو جاتا۔ اسلئے کہ ملک یمین سے ملک نکاح فاسد ہو جاتا ہے اور دوسری صورت میں خرید کردہ غلام آزاد ہو جاتا کیونکہ رسول اللہ صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا ہے:

اب اگر ہم نے جہت اولی کا منتبر کرتے ہوئے ملک اور حقوق دونوں وکیل کیلئے ثابت کر دیجیسا کہ قیاس کا تقاضہ ہے تو مؤکل کا اس کو وکیل کرنا باطل ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں وکیل، وکیل نہیں رہے گا بلکہ اصل ہو جائے گا۔ حالانکہ وکیل تصرف میں موکل کا نائب ہے اور اصیل نہیں ہے اور اگر ملک اور حقوق دونوں موکل کیلئے ثابت کر دیں تو وکیل کا عقد کیلئے کلام کرنا باطل ہو جائے گا۔ حالانکہ عقد وکیل ہی کے کلام سے حاصل ہوا ہے۔ پس دونوں جہتوں کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے ہم نے ملک تو مؤکل کیلئے ثابت کی ہے۔ کیونکہ وکیل بنانے سے ملکیت حاصل کرنا ہی مقصود ہوتا ہے۔ اس کی طرف مصنف ہدایہ نے اعتبارا للتوكيل السابق سے اشارہ کیا ہے اور حقوق وکیل کیلئے ثابت کہتے ہیں ۔ پس ثابت ہو گیا عقد کی اس قسم اول میں حقوق وکیل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوتے ہیں۔ اور موکل کے ساتھ متعلق نہیں ہوتے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ مسئلہ عیب میں قدرے تفصیل ہے جس کو ہم انشاء اللہ باب الوكالة بالبيع والشراء كے تحت بيان کریں گئے۔ فانتظروا انى معكم من المنتظرين
The author of Al-Hidayah said: This method of establishing ownership for the principal is the method of Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, and most Hanafis have approved it. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said the method is more correct, and the author of Al-Hidayah also said it is correct. But Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said: Due to purchase, ownership initially establishes for the agent, then transfers to the principal. So when according to Imam al-Karkhi ownership initially establishes for the agent, the rights of the contract as subsidiary to ownership also establish for the agent, not for the principal. In summary, according to Shaykh Abu Tahir al-Dabbas, ownership due to the agent's purchase of something establishes initially for the principal, but not with originality; rather in the capacity of the agent's deputyship. And according to Imam al-Karkhi, ownership initially establishes for the agent, then transfers to the principal.
The proof for the correctness of Abu Tahir's statement is: If the agent purchases his own married woman who is a slave of another, or purchases any of his close blood relatives who is a slave of another, then neither does this marriage become invalidated nor does that close relative become free. This shows that ownership does not establish for the purchaser, i.e., the agent; rather ownership establishes initially for the principal, although it establishes in the capacity of the agent's deputyship. Because if ownership had established for the agent, then in the first instance his marriage would become invalid, since possession (milk yamin) invalidates marriage, and in the second instance the purchased slave would become free, because the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said: "Whoever possesses a close blood relative, that relative becomes free."
The authors of Fath al-Qadir and Al-Inayah, in response to Imam al-Shafi'i's evidence and to establish the rights of the contract for the agent, stated the evidence in this manner: The agent's transaction has two aspects: (1) The transaction is achieved by the agent's speech, (2) The agent is the deputy of the principal in the transaction.
Now, if we take the first aspect and establish both ownership and rights for the agent, as analogy (qiyas) requires, then the principal's appointment of him as agent would become void, because in this case the agent would not remain an agent but would become the principal (asl). While the agent is the deputy of the principal in the transaction, not the principal himself. And if we establish both ownership and rights for the principal, then the agent's speech for the contract would become invalid, while the contract is achieved by the agent's speech. Therefore, considering both aspects, we have established ownership for the principal, because the purpose of appointing an agent is to acquire ownership. The author of Al-Hidayah indicated this with the phrase "اعتبارا للتوكيل السابق" (considering the prior agency). And the rights are established for the agent. So it is established that in this first category of contract, rights pertain to the agent, not to the principal. The author of Al-Hidayah said that in the matter of defects (aib) there is some detail which we will mention, God willing, under the chapter of agency for sale and purchase. So wait, I am with you among those who wait.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two prominent early Hanafi jurists, Abu Tahir al-Dabbas and Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi, regarding the ownership of property purchased by an agent (wakil). The author of Al-Hidayah sides with Abu Tahir, as do most Hanafis. According to Abu Tahir, ownership is established directly for the principal from the moment of purchase, though through the agency of the wakil. Al-Karkhi holds that ownership first belongs to the agent and then transfers to the principal. The passage then provides a strong proof for Abu Tahir's view: if the agent buys his own wife who is a slave, the marriage remains valid despite the agent now owning her, because ownership for the agent would have invalidated it. Similarly, buying a close relative who is a slave does not free him, because ownership for the agent would trigger emancipation. These outcomes demonstrate that ownership never rests with the agent. The passage then addresses an objection from Imam al-Shafi'i through the intermediary of commentators (Sahib Fath al-Qadir and Sahib al-Inayah), who explain that the agent's action has two dimensions: his speech effectuates the contract, and he acts as a deputy for the principal. To give effect to both, ownership is given to the principal (since agency aims at that), but rights like option clauses pertain to the agent. This balance is indicated by the Hidayah's phrase 'اعتبارا للتوكيل السابق'. The passage concludes by noting that details regarding defects in the purchased item will be discussed later. The dispute is resolved in favor of Abu Tahir's position, which is the relied-upon view.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A close blood relative whom one cannot marry, such as father, brother, etc.
ملک یمین (milk yamin): Ownership through purchase or possession, as opposed to marriage (milk nikah).
نیابت (niyabah): Deputyship; the capacity in which the agent acts on behalf of the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wakil
B) The principal
C) Both share it
D) The seller until delivery

Key Takeaway
Ownership of property purchased by an agent belongs directly to the principal, never to the agent, as proven by the hadith on emancipation of close relatives.

Invalidity of Agency for Borrowing (Tawkil bil Istiqrad)
Ruling on agency for borrowing with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tawkil bil istiqrad and explain the two reasons why it is invalid, including the qiyas on the invalid agency for selling another's property.

Core Idea
Appointing an agent to borrow on your behalf is invalid because it forces the agent to assume liability while you take the benefit.

Agency is a valid contract, but what if you ask someone to borrow money for you? The Hidayah reveals a subtle distinction: if the agent merely conveys a request, it is valid; but if you appoint him as an agent to borrow, making him liable for repayment, the agency is invalid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this Hanafi ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts agency for receiving (valid, agent is messenger) with tawkil bil istiqrad. Identify the key difference: in the former, rights attach to principal; in the latter, the agent assumes liability.
2
Extract the two reasons for invalidity
First reason: ordering disposal of another's property is not permissible. Second reason: qiyas on invalid agency for sale with condition that price goes to principal.
The agent would be making himself liable for the consideration while the principal gets the benefit.
3
Trace the qiyas
Follow the analogy: asl = invalid sale agency, hukm = batil, illa = agent assumes liability for consideration while benefit goes to principal, far' = tawkil bil istiqrad, resulting hukm = batil.
The qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is manifest.
4
Apply the consequence
If the agent nevertheless borrows, he becomes the owner of the loaned property and bears the risk of loss. The principal does not become owner.
However, if someone is appointed as a mere messenger (safir) to convey a request, it is valid and ownership goes to the principal.

صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اگر ہبہ، صدقہ ، عاریہ ، ودیعت، رہن، قرضہ مانگنے والے کی طرف سے وکیل ہو تو بھی وہ سفیر ہے۔ یعنی اس صورت میں بھی حقوق موکل کے ساتھ متعلق ہوں گے نہ کہ وکیل کے ساتھ ۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ قرضہ مانگنے کیلئے وکیل کرنا باطل سے یعنی اگر ایک شخص نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ تو میرے لئے فلاں سے قرض لے آاور قرض مانگنے میں تو میرا وکیل ہے تو یہ تو کیل باطل ہے کیونکہ اس صورت میں موکل نے وکیل کو دوسرے کے مال میں تصرف کا امر کیا ہے۔ حالانکہ دوسرے کے مال میں تصرف کا حکم کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ قرضہ لینے والا قرضہ کا بدل اپنے ذمہ لازم کرتا ہے۔ پس قرضہ مانگنے کیلئے وکیل بنانے کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مؤکل وکیل سے کہتا ہے کہ تو بدل قرض مثلاً ایک سو روپیہ اپنے ذمہ لازم کرے اور اس کا عوض باطل یعنی مال قرض میرے لئے ہوگا۔ یعنی قرض تو میرے لئے ہوگا اور اس کا بدل تجھ پر لازم ہوگا۔ یہ ایسا ہو گیا جیسے کوئی شخص دوسرے سے کہے کہ فلاں آدمی کی فلان چیز فروخت کر دے اس شرط پر کہ اس کا شمن میرے لئے ہوگا۔ حالانکہ یہ تو کیل بہالہیچ باطل ہے۔ پس اسی پر قیاس کر کے تو کیل بالاستقراض بھی باطل ہے اور جب قرضہ مانگنے کیلئے وکیل کرنا باطل ہے تو اگر وکیل نے قرضہ مانگ بھی لیا تو اس کا مالک مؤکل نہ ہوگا۔ بلکہ خود وکیل ہوگا اور اگر وکیل کے پاس سے مال قرض ہلاک ہو گیا تو وہ وکیل کا مال ہلاک ہوگا نہ کہ موکل کا مال اور اگر قرضہ کیلئے کسی کو نیچی بنایا تو درست ہے۔ مثلاً ایک شخص دوسرے سے کہتا ہے کہ مجھ کو فلاں آدمی نے تیرے پاس قرضہ لینے کیلئے بھیجا ہے۔ تو یہ درست ہے چنانچہ مقرض ( مرسل الیہ ) نے اگر قرضہ دے دیا تو قرضہ مانگنے والے یعنی مرسل کیلئے ملک ثابت ہو جائے گی۔
The author of al-Hidayah says: If there is an agent from the side of the one requesting a gift, charity, loan (ariyah), deposit, pledge, or loan (qard), then even then he is a messenger. That is, in this case too, the rights are attached to the principal, not to the agent. However, it is necessary that appointing an agent for requesting a loan (tawkil bil istiqrad) is invalid. That is, if one person says to another: 'Go and borrow from so-and-so for me, and you are my agent in borrowing,' then this agency is invalid. Because in this case, the principal has ordered the agent to dispose of another's property, while it is not permissible to order disposal of another's property. A second reason is that the borrower makes the consideration for the loan incumbent upon his own liability. So the meaning of appointing an agent for borrowing is that the principal tells the agent: 'Make the consideration for the loan, e.g., one hundred rupees, incumbent upon your liability, and its exchange (the loaned property) will be for me.' That is, the loan will be for me, and its consideration will be upon you. This is like a person saying to another: 'Sell such-and-such item of so-and-so's property on the condition that its price will be for me.' This agency is completely invalid. By analogy, tawkil bil istiqrad is also invalid. When appointing an agent for requesting a loan is invalid, if the agent does borrow, the principal does not become owner; rather, the agent himself becomes owner. If the borrowed property is destroyed in the agent's possession, it is the agent's property that is destroyed, not the principal's. But if someone is made a messenger (safir) for a loan, it is valid. For example, a person says to another: 'So-and-so has sent me to you to take a loan.' This is valid. If the lender (the one sent to) gives the loan, ownership is established for the one who sent (the principal).
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage from the Hidayah commentary addresses two related issues. First, it confirms that even when the agent is appointed by the recipient (e.g., the donee or borrower) to receive the property, the agent remains a mere messenger (safir) and contractual rights attach to the principal, not to the agent. Second, it discusses the specific case of tawkil bil istiqrad (appointing an agent to request a loan from another). The commentator rules that such agency is batil (invalid) for two reasons: (1) the principal cannot order the agent to dispose of another's property without permission, and (2) the agent would be making himself liable for the consideration while the principal gets the benefit, which is unjust and akin to an invalid transaction. Therefore, if the agent nevertheless borrows, he becomes the owner of the loaned money and bears the risk of loss. However, if someone is appointed simply as a messenger (safir) to convey a request for a loan (e.g., 'I have been sent to borrow from you'), this is valid, and the loan ownership goes to the principal. This distinction is crucial in practice: a valid loan agency requires the agent to act only as a conduit, not as a party assuming liability.

Key Terms
استقراض (istiqrad): Asking for a loan; in this context, it means the act of requesting and receiving a loan on behalf of another.
باطل (batil): Legally invalid and void ab initio; no rights or obligations arise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hidayah commentary, why is tawkil bil istiqrad (appointing an agent to request a loan) invalid?
A) Because the agent cannot speak on behalf of the principal.
B) Because it involves ordering the agent to dispose of another's property and makes the agent liable for the consideration.
C) Because the principal must be present during the loan transaction.
D) Because the Quran prohibits borrowing through an agent.

Key Takeaway
Tawkil bil istiqrad is invalid because it forces the agent to be liable for the loan repayment while the principal enjoys the benefit, analogous to an invalid sale agency.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Muqasah (Set-off) When Buyer Owes Both Principal and Agent: The Hanafi Dispute
Explains the ruling on muqasah between principal and buyer with proofs and Abu Yusuf's objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi majority ruling on muqasah when a buyer owes both principal and agent, explain the proofs (qiyas on ibra and istihsan), and evaluate Imam Abu Yusuf's objection.

Core Idea
The agent can waive his right to collect the price (muqasah), but he becomes guarantor for it.

In sales through an agent (wakil), the buyer may owe both the principal and the agent. The question arises: can the agent set off (muqasah) the price against a debt the principal owes the buyer? This passage presents the Hanafi majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and Imam Abu Yusuf's dissent, along with the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenario
Identify the parties: buyer owes agent the price (thaman), and buyer and principal have mutual debts. The dispute is whether the agent can effect muqasah between the buyer and principal.
The agent has the exclusive right to take possession (qabd) of the price.
2
State the majority ruling
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, muqasah occurs between the principal and the buyer. The agent becomes guarantor (damin) for the price to the principal.
If buyer owes only the agent, muqasah is against the agent's debt.
3
Trace the majority's proofs
First proof (qiyas): muqasah is ibra bil-iwad, analogized to ibra bila-iwad where only the principal's discharge is effective. Second proof (istihsan): set-off against the principal's debt avoids the agent having to pay from his own pocket.
The a fortiori argument: if the agent can discharge without consideration (ibra), he can discharge with consideration (muqasah).
4
Present Abu Yusuf's objection
Imam Abu Yusuf argues that the price is the principal's property; the agent cannot dispose of it without permission. Therefore, ibra and muqasah are both invalid.
He analogizes to the agent gifting the price to the buyer, which is prohibited.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical difference: majority allows muqasah with agent assuming liability; Abu Yusuf forbids it. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the majority view.
The agent's guarantee ensures the principal is not harmed.

قرض ہے تو ایک ہزار روپی شمن اور وہ ایک ہزار روپیہ جو موکل پر قرض ہے ان کے درمیان مقاصہ واقع ہو گا۔ یعنی مؤکل اور مشتری دونوں برابر سرابر ہو جائیں گے۔ نہ موکل کا مشتری پر کچھ رہے گا اور نہ مشتری کا موکل پر کچھ رہے گا اور مؤکل کو وکیل سے کچھ من وغیرہ لینے کا حق بھی نہ ہوگا۔

دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ مذکورہ صورت میں اگر وکیل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیں تو اس صورت میں اس کی ضرورت پڑے گی کہ وکیل اپنے پاس سے مؤکل کوشن ادا کرے اور اگر مؤکل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیں تو اس کی ضرورت پیش نہ آئے گی ۔ پس مسافت کو کم کرنے کیلئے موکل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ قرار دیا گیا اور اگر مشتری کا قرضہ تنہا وکیل پر ہو تو اس صورت میں طرفین کے نزد یک وکیل کے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقاصہ واقع ہوگا۔ کیونکہ وکیل طرفین کے نزدیک مشری کو شمن سے بلا عوض بری کر دینے کا مالک ہے اور جب وکیل بلاعوض مشتری کوشن سے بری کر دیتے کا مالک ہے تو مقاصہ یعنی ابراء بالعوض کا مالک بدرجہ اولی ہو گا ۔ پس ایراء بالعوض کو ابراء بلاعوض پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے۔ ہاں اتنی بات ضرور ہے کہ وکیل مشتری کو بلاعوض بری کرے یا بعوض بری کرے دونوں صورتوں میں وکیل اپنے مؤکل کیلئے مقدار شمن کا ضامن ہوگا۔
رہی یہ بات کہ طرفین کے نزدیک وکیل بلا عوض مشتری کو شمن سے بری کر دینے کا مالک کیوں ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ وکیل کے حق میں ابراء نام ہے قبضہ کے حق کو ساقط کرنے کا اور قبضہ خالص وکیل کا حق ہے۔ چنانچہ مؤکل اگر وکیل کو قبضہ کرنے سے منع کرنا چاہے تو اس کو یہ اختیار نہ ہوگا اور اگر مؤکل خود قبضہ کرنا چاہے تو اس کو یہ حق بھی نہیں ہے۔ پس وکیل ہمشتری کو قبضہ ثمن سے بری کرنے میں خالص اپنے حق کو ساقط کرنے والا ہے اور اپنا حق ساقط کرنے کا ہر شخص مجاز ہے۔ لہذا وکیل مشتری کو بری کرنے کا مجاز ہے اور جب وکیل بلا عوض مشتری کو بری کرنے کا مالک نے تو بالعوض بری کرنے یعنی مقاصہ کرنے کا حق ساقط کرنے کا بدرجہ اولی مالک و مختار ہوگا۔ مگر چونکہ وکیل نے قبضہ کرنے کا حق ساقط کر دیا۔ اور اسکی وجہ سے مؤکل کیلئے شن وصول کرنے کا دروازہ بند ہو گیا ۔ کیونکہ مؤکل کو مشتری کی طرف سے شمن پر قبضہ کرنے کا کوئی حق نہیں ہوتا اسلئے وکیل، موکل کیلئے مقدار ثمن کا ضامن ہوگا۔ جیسے راہن اگر اپنے عبد مرہون کو آزاد کر دے تو وہ آزاد ہو جائیگا۔ کیونکہ عہد مرہون راہن ہی کی ملک ہے۔ اگر چہ مرتہن کے قبضہ میں ہے لیکن چونکہ غلام کی مالیت سے مرتین کیلئے اپنا قرضہ وصول کرنا ناممکن ہو گیا۔ اسلئے راہن، مرتہن کیلئے مقدار قرض کا ضامن ہوگا۔ حضرت امام ابو یوسف نے فرمایا کہ وکیل مشتری کو بلاعوض بری کر دینے کا مالک نہیں ہے کیونکہ مشتری کے ذمہ میں جوشن ہے وہ مؤکل کی ملک ہے پس وکیل کا مشتری کو بری کرنا غیر یعنی مؤکل کی ملک میں بلا اجازت تصرف کرنا ہے اور بلا اجازت کسی کی ملک میں تصرف کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ لہذا وکیل کا مشتری کو بری کرنا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا۔ جیسے وکیل کاشن پر قبضہ کر کے پھر مشتری کو ہبہ کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ بہر حال جب وکیل کیلئے ابراء بلا عوض جائز نہیں ہے تو ابراء بالعوض یعنی مقاصہ بھی اسی پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے جائز نہ ہوگا۔
If there is a debt [on the buyer to the agent] of one thousand rupees and the one thousand rupees that the principal owes [to the buyer], muqasah (set-off) will occur between them. That is, the principal and the buyer will be equal; neither will the principal owe anything to the buyer nor the buyer to the principal, and the principal will have no right to claim anything from the agent. The proof for this is that muqasah is ibra (discharge) for consideration, and it is analogized to ibra without consideration. If both principal and agent together discharge the buyer from the price, the buyer is discharged by the principal's discharge, not by the agent's. Thus, in that case, the principal cannot refer back to the agent for the price. Similarly, when in ibra without consideration the principal's discharge is effective, not the agent's, then in ibra with consideration (muqasah) the discharge and set-off will be effective in exchange for the principal's debt, not the agent's. The second proof is that in the mentioned case, if they set off against the agent's debt, it would require the agent to pay the principal from his own pocket, but if they set off against the principal's debt, no such need arises. So to reduce difficulty, set-off is made against the principal's debt. However, if the buyer owes the agent alone, then according to both [sides], set-off will be against the agent's debt, because the agent, according to both, owns the right to discharge the buyer from the price without consideration. And when he owns that, he owns muqasah (discharge with consideration) a fortiori. Therefore, discharge with consideration is analogized to discharge without consideration. But note that whether the agent discharges the buyer without consideration or with consideration, in both cases the agent is guarantor for the amount of the price to the principal. As for why the agent owns the right to discharge the buyer without consideration according to both sides, the answer is that in the agent's right, ibra is the dropping of the right of qabd (possession), and qabd is exclusively the agent's right. Thus, if the principal wants to prevent the agent from taking possession, he cannot; and if the principal wants to take possession himself, he has no right. So when the agent discharges the buyer from the price, he is merely dropping his own right, and everyone is permitted to drop his own right. Hence the agent is permitted to discharge the buyer. And when he owns that, he owns discharge with consideration (muqasah) a fortiori. But since the agent has dropped the right of qabd, the door for the principal to receive the price is closed, because the principal has no right to take the price from the buyer. Therefore, the agent is guarantor for the amount of the price to the principal, just as if a pledgor frees his pledged slave, the slave is free, and the pledgor becomes guarantor for the amount of the loan to the pledgee. Imam Abu Yusuf has stated that the agent does not own the right to discharge the buyer without consideration, because what is in the buyer's liability (the price) is the principal's property. So the agent's discharging the buyer is a disposition in someone else's (the principal's) property without permission, which is not allowed. Therefore, the agent's discharging the buyer would not be valid, just as it is not valid for the agent to take possession of the price and then gift it to the buyer. In any case, when the agent is not permitted to discharge without consideration, then by analogy, discharge with consideration (muqasah) also would not be valid.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning muqasah (set-off) when an agent sells an item and the buyer owes both the principal and the agent. The author first presents the ruling of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani): if the buyer owes the agent the price and also owes the principal (or the principal owes the buyer), set-off occurs between the principal and the buyer, not the agent. The majority offer two proofs. First, muqasah is ibra (discharge) with consideration, and it is analogized to ibra without consideration. In ibra without consideration, only the principal's discharge is effective because the debt belongs to him. Therefore, in muqasah, only the principal's debt is considered for set-off. Second, set-off against the principal's debt avoids the extra step of the agent having to pay the principal from his own pocket, which follows istihsan. However, if the buyer owes only the agent, then set-off occurs against the agent's debt, because the agent owns the right to discharge the buyer without consideration (since he owns the right of possession). The agent may waive his right, but he then becomes guarantor for the price to the principal, analogized to a pledgor freeing a pledged slave. Imam Abu Yusuf dissents, arguing that the price belongs to the principal, so the agent cannot discharge the buyer without explicit permission. He analogizes this to the agent gifting the price to the buyer, which is invalid. Consequently, muqasah would also be invalid. The passage thus clarifies that the majority view permits the agent to discharge and set off where appropriate, with the agent assuming liability.

Key Terms
مقاصہ (muqāṣah): Set-off; canceling mutual debts between two parties, treated as ibra (discharge) with consideration.
ابراء (ibrāʾ): Discharge from a debt; in the context of wakalah, it refers to waiving the right to demand payment.
قبضہ (qabḍah): Possession; the right to take physical possession of the price from the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is the ruling when the buyer owes both the agent and the principal, and the agent sells an item?
A) Set-off occurs between the agent and the buyer, and the principal has no claim.
B) Set-off occurs between the principal and the buyer, and the agent has no claim against the principal.
C) The agent must pay the principal before any set-off.
D) No set-off is possible because the debts are different.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi muamalat, an agent's exclusive right of possession (qabd) empowers him to discharge the buyer (muqasah), but he becomes responsible for the price to the principal.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conditions for Valid Agency in Purchase
States the specification requirement for purchase agency and the exception of general agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two valid ways to specify the subject matter in a limited agency for purchase and explain why a general agency is valid despite apparent uncertainty.

Core Idea
A limited agency needs genus + description or genus + max price; a general agency is valid because delegation to judgment tolerates slight ignorance.

This lesson extracts the key condition for valid agency in purchase. You will learn that the principal must eliminate fatal ignorance by specifying the item's genus and one of two alternatives. The exception of general agency shows the Hanafi principle of flexibility (tawsiʿah) in agency, allowing the agent to use his discretion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of agency
Determine whether the principal gave a limited agency or a general agency. A general agency is indicated by phrases like 'Buy for me what you see fit', delegating the judgment to the agent.
2
Check specification for limited agency
If the agency is limited, verify whether the principal specified either (a) the genus and description of the item, or (b) the genus and the maximum price. Only these two combinations suffice.
Specifying only the price or only a vague quality is insufficient.
3
Apply the ruling
If the specification is present, the agency is valid and the agent must act accordingly. If absent, the agency is invalid (fāsid) and purchases made by the agent do not bind the principal.
If the agency is general, no specification is needed; any purchase made by the agent in good faith is valid.
4
Recognize the underlying usul principle
Understand that the Hanafi school tolerates slight ignorance (jihālah yasīrah) in agency by istiḥsān, because agency is built on flexibility and assistance, and imposing strict conditions would cause hardship.
This principle is the basis for validating a general agency and accepting a description-based specification even if not every detail is known.

فصل في الشراء
ترجمہ یہ باب خرید و فروخت کی وکالت کے بیان میں سے پہلی فصل خریدنے کے بیان میں ۔
تشریح چونکہ وکالت کے ابواب میں سب سے زیادہ کثیر الوقوع اور سب سے زیادہ ضرورت کی چیز وکالت بالبيع والشراء ہے۔ اسلئے وكالت بالبيع والشراء کے احکام پہلے ذکر کئے گئے۔ اور شراء کے ذریعہ چونکہ ملک مبیع کا اثبات ہوتا ہے۔ اور بیع کے ذریعہ اس کا ازالہ ہوتا ہے اور ازالہ، اثبات کے بعد ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے شراء سے متعلق احکام پہلے ذکر کئے گئے اور بیچ کی وکالت کے احکام بعد میں ذکر کئے گئے ہیں۔ علامہ ابن الہمام نے فصل فی الشراء کی اس وجہ تقدیم کو پسند نہیں فرمایا اور نا پسندیدگی کی وجہ یہ بیان فرمائی کہ شرا، جس طرح صبیح کے اندر اثبات ملک کی خبر دیتا ہے۔ اسی طرح شمن سے ملک زائل کرنے کی خبر بھی دیتا ہے اور بیع جس طرح مبیع سے ملک زائل کرنے کی خبر دیتا ہے۔ اسی طرح شمن میں اثبات ملک کی خبر بھی دیتا ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے علما ء نے کہا ہے کہ شراء جالب المبیع اور سالب للثمن ہے اور بیچ جالب للثمن اور سالب السمیع ہے یعنی شراء کے ذریعہ میچ کو حاصل کیا جاتا ہے اور شمن کو اپنی ملک سے نکالا جاتا ہے۔ بہر حال اثبات اور ازالہ پر دلالت کرنے میں شراء اور بیچ دونوں برابر ہیں اور جب دونوں برابر ہیں تو وکالت بالشراء کو مقدم کرنے کی کوئی وجہ نہیں ہے۔ مناسب وجہ یہ ہے کہ شراء کے مسائل بھی کثیر الوقوع ہیں اور ان کی ضرورت بھی زیادہ پیش آتی ہے۔ کیونکہ اکثر لوگ دوسرے کو اپنے کھانے پینے اور پہننے کی چیزوں کو خریدنے کیلئے وکیل بناتے ہیں اور فروخت کرنے کیلئے اس درجہ میں وکیل نہیں بناتے۔ پس وکالت بالشراء کے احکام چونکہ کثیر الوقوع ہیں اور ان کی ضرورت بھی زیادہ پڑتی ہے، اسلئے وکالت بالشراء کے احکام کو وکالت بالسمیع کے احکام پر مقدم کیا گیا۔
وکیل بالشراء کو جنس اور صفت یا جنس اور انتہائی کشمن بتانا ضروری ہیں
ومن وكل رجلا بشراء شيء فلابد من تسمية جنسه وصفته او جنسه و مبلغ ثمنه ليصير الفعل المؤكل به معلوما فيمكنه الايتمار الا ان يوكله وكالة عامة فيقول ابتع لى ما رأيت لانه فوض الأمر الى رأيه فاى شىء يشتريه يكون ممتثلا والاصل فيه ان الجهالة اليسيرة تتحمل في الوكالة كجهالة الوصف استحسانا لان مبنى التوكيل على التوسعة لانه استعانة وفى اعتبار لهذا الشرط بعض الحرج وهو مدفوع ترجمہ اور اگر کسی نے کسی آدمی کو کسی چیز کے خریدنے کا وکیل کیا تو اس شی ء کی جنس اور صفت یا اس کی جنس اور اس کا انتہائی شمن بیان کرنا ضروری ہے تا کہ جس کام کیلئے وکیل کیا گیا ہے وہ معلوم ہو سکے اور تحمیل حکم ممکن ہو سکے۔ مگر یہ کہ اس کو وکالت عامہ سے وکیل کرے اور کہے کہ میرے لئے وہ چیز خرید لے جس کو تو مناسب خیال کرے اسلئے کہ مؤکل نے معاملہ اس کی رائے کے سپرد کر دیا ہے۔ پس جس چیز کو وہ خریدے گا حکم کی تعمیل قرار پائے گا اور اس میں ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ وکالت میں استحسانا تھوڑی سی جہالت برداشت ہو جاتی ہے جیسے وصف کا
Chapter of Agency for Sale and Purchase / Section on Purchase. Translation: This chapter is about agency for sale and purchase; the first section is about purchase. Commentary: Since among the chapters of agency, agency for sale and purchase is the most frequent and most needed, its rulings are mentioned first. And since purchase establishes ownership of the sold item, and sale removes it, and removal comes after establishment, the rulings related to purchase are mentioned first, and rulings of agency for sale are mentioned later. Ibn al-Humam did not like this reason for precedence, stating that purchase, just as it informs of establishing ownership over the sold item, also informs of removing ownership from the price; and sale, just as it informs of removing ownership from the sold item, also informs of establishing ownership in the price. For this reason, scholars have said that purchase brings the sold item and releases the price, and sale brings the price and releases the sold item. Thus, both purchase and sale are equal in indicating establishment and removal. When they are equal, there is no reason to prefer agency for purchase. The appropriate reason is that the issues of purchase are frequent and their need arises more often, because most people appoint agents to buy items for food, drink, and clothing, not so much for selling. So because the rulings of agency for purchase are frequent and more needed, they are prioritized over agency for sale. / It is necessary for the agent to purchase to be told the genus and description or the genus and the maximum price. / And whoever appoints an agent to purchase something, it is necessary to specify its genus and description, or its genus and its maximum price, so that the act entrusted becomes known and he can comply, unless he appoints him by a general agency, saying, 'Buy for me what you see fit,' because he has delegated the matter to his judgment; whatever he buys will be in compliance. The principle is that slight ignorance is tolerated in agency, such as ignorance of description, by istihsan because agency is built on flexibility, as it is seeking assistance, and imposing this condition would cause some hardship, which is repelled. Translation: And if someone appoints a person as agent to purchase something, it is necessary to mention its genus and description, or its genus and its maximum price, so that the act for which he is appointed becomes known and compliance is possible, unless he appoints him with a general agency and says, 'Buy for me whatever you consider suitable,' because the principal has entrusted the matter to his judgment. So whatever he buys will be considered compliant. The principle is that slight ignorance is tolerated in agency by istihsan, like ignorance of description.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on agency for sale and purchase (Wakalah bi al-Bay' wa al-Shira'). It first explains why the section on purchase (Fasl fi al-Shira') is placed before that on sale. The main reason given is practical: people more often appoint agents to buy things for daily needs than to sell. Ibn al-Humam criticized the earlier justification that purchase establishes ownership and sale removes it, noting that both actions involve both establishment and removal. The author then presents the core ruling: for a valid agency to purchase, the principal must specify either the genus and description of the item, or the genus and the maximum price. This is necessary so the agent knows what he is authorized to do. However, an exception exists if the principal gives a general agency (wakalah 'ammah), saying 'Buy for me what you see fit,' in which case the agent has discretion and any purchase is valid. The underlying principle is that slight ignorance (jihalah yasirah) is tolerated in agency due to istihsān, because agency is based on flexibility and avoiding hardship. Thus, even if the description is not fully given, the agency can still be valid.

Key Terms
وکالت بالشراء (wakālah bi al-shirāʾ): Agency to purchase; the principal authorizes the agent to buy a specific item on his behalf.
جنس و صفت (jins wa ṣifah): Genus and description; the category and qualities of the item to be purchased.
وکالت عامہ (wakālah ʿāmmah): General agency; the principal authorizes the agent to act according to his discretion without specifying details.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the requirement for a valid limited agency to purchase (not general)?
A) Only the price must be specified.
B) The agent must be told the exact item by name.
C) The genus and description, or genus and maximum price must be specified.
D) No specification is needed; the agent can buy anything.

Key Takeaway
A limited agency for purchase requires specification of genus and description or genus and max price; a general agency bypasses this via delegation to judgment, based on the principle of tolerating slight ignorance.
